فصل في الحقيقة والمجاز
ونصابٌ من الدراهم يُصرفُ الدينُ إلى الدراهم، حتى لو حال عليهما الحولُ تحب
الزكاةُ عنده في نصاب الغنم، ولا تجب في الدراهم.
أي محمد
ولو ترجح بعضُ وُجوه المشترك ببيان من قبل المتكلم كان مفسراً. وحكمه: أنه يجب العَملُ به يقيناً.
مثاله: إذا قال: "الفلان" على عشرة دراهم من نقد بخارى" فقوله: "من نقد بخارى" تفسير له، فلولا ذلك لكان منصرفاً إلى غالب نقد البلد بطريق التأويل، فيترجح المفسر
المذكور من الدراهم
فلا يجب نقد البلد.
بل يجب نقد بخاری
The first investigation in the Book of Allah. Section on the Common and the Interpreted. The ruling of the interpreted is the obligation to act upon it with the possibility of error. And that is so. Its example in legal matters is what we said: if the price is left unspecified in a sale, it is based on the dominant currency of the town, specifying the dominant currency by way of interpretation. If the currencies were different, the sale is void for what we mentioned. And the interpretation of 'aqra' as menses, and the interpretation of 'nikah' in the verse as intercourse, and the interpretation of kinayah (allusive expressions) in the context of discussing divorce as divorce, from the common between contract and intercourse, and about divorce, are of this kind. And based on this we said: the debt that prevents zakat is directed to the easier of the two properties for repayment of the debt. And Muhammad furrara (branched) upon this and said: 'If a man marries a woman with a nisab (zakatable amount) as dower, and he owns a nisab of sheep, the debt is directed to the easier of the two concerning the zakat nisab absolutely, so understand with the possibility of error in its interpretation; because interpretation is only by a speculative matter, and it entails the possibility of error; because the mujtahid may err and be correct. In some copies, the wording is 'ala instead of ma'a, but 'ala then means ma'a (with), as it is said: so-and-so is a sea of knowledge despite his youth, i.e., with his youth.' ... The sale becomes void due to impossibility of combination and lack of preponderance unless one of them is specified, then the ambiguity leading to dispute is removed, and the voidness was only for that. And the interpretation of 'aqra' in the verse of waiting period, which is His statement: 'And divorced women shall wait for three quru'' [al-Shafi: p.63]. And the interpretation of kinayah: i.e., in a man's statement to his wife: 'You are ba'in' [al-Shafi: p.63] is of this kind, i.e., of the category of interpretation; because quru' is common between menses and purity, and nikah is common between intercourse and contract, and the words of kinayah like ba'in are common, possibly from bayan (separation) or from bayn (distinctness). So when quru' is interpreted as menses, nikah as intercourse, and kinayah in divorce context as the intention of separation from the bond of marriage, that is interpretation [as in the commentaries]. And upon this: i.e., that which is possible for two things is directed to one of its possibilities by evidence that gives preponderance of opinion, as in common terms. To the easier of the two properties: i.e., if a man has nisabs of dirhams, dinars, goods, and grazing animals, and he owes a debt that consumes part of them, it is first directed to cash because repaying the debt from it is easier for not needing to sell, then to goods because they are for trade, then to grazing animals because they are surplus to need, then to occupied property like a dwelling house, body garments, riding animals, and service slaves, and others. The first investigation in the Book of Allah. Section on the Literal and Figurative. And a nisab of dirhams: the debt is directed to the dirhams, so that if a year passed over both, zakat is due according to him on the nisab of sheep, and not on the dirhams. i.e., Muhammad. And if some aspects of the common are given preponderance by a clarification from the speaker, it is mufassar (explicated). Its ruling: it is obligatory to act upon it with certainty. Its example: if he says: 'I owe so-and-so ten dirhams from the currency of Bukhara', his statement 'from the currency of Bukhara' is an explication of it. Otherwise, it would have been directed to the dominant currency of the town by way of interpretation. So the mufassar mentioned of the dirhams is preponderant, so the country's currency is not obligatory, but rather the currency of Bukhara is obligatory.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage begins a new section on mushtarak (common term) and mu'awwal (interpreted term). The author states the ruling of mu'awwal: it is obligatory to act upon it, but with the possibility of error because the preponderance is based on speculative evidence. He then gives several examples from fiqh: if the price currency is unspecified in a sale, it is interpreted by the dominant currency of the town; if there are multiple currencies, the sale becomes void due to impossibility of combination and lack of preponderance. Another example is interpreting 'quru' in the Quran (2:228) as menses rather than purity, and 'nikah' in the verse as intercourse rather than the contract, and the use of kinayah (allusive expressions) like 'ba'in' during divorce discussions as meaning divorce. He then derives a fiqhi corollary: the debt that prevents zakat should be directed to the easier of the two properties for repayment. He then quotes Muhammad al-Shaybani's branch case: if a man marries with a nisab of dirhams as dower and also owns a nisab of sheep, the debt is directed to the easier property, so zakat is due on the sheep but not on the dirhams because the dirhams are occupied by the dower debt. The passage then defines mufassar: if the speaker gives clarification that preponderates one aspect of a common term, that term becomes mufassar and must be acted upon with certainty. The example is specifying currency as from Bukhara.

Key Terms
المؤول (al-mu'awwal): اللفظ المشترك الذي رجح أحد معانيه بدليل ظني
المفسر (al-mufassar): اللفظ المشترك الذي رجح أحد معانيه بدليل قاطع من المتكلم
المشترك (al-mushtarak): اللفظ الموضوع لمعنيين أو أكثر وضعا حقيقيا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling of acting upon mu'awwal (the interpreted term)?
A) Obligatory with certainty
B) Obligatory with possibility of error
C) Prohibited unless clarified
D) Recommended but not required

Key Takeaway
Mu'awwal is a common term given preponderance by speculative evidence; it must be acted upon but admits the possibility of error.

Haqiqah and Majaz: Definitions and the Rule of Non-Combination
Defines haqiqah and majaz and argues they cannot be combined.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define haqiqah and majaz, state the Hanafi rule that they cannot be intended together, and apply the rule by identifying when a word is used metaphorically.

Core Idea
A single word cannot simultaneously convey its literal and metaphorical meanings—the diverting clue excludes the literal.

This passage from Usul al-Shashi introduces two fundamental categories of word meaning in Islamic legal theory: haqiqah (literal truth) and majaz (metaphor). It not only defines them but also argues that they cannot be combined in a single instance of usage. Understanding this principle is crucial for interpreting revealed texts correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definitions
Read the passage's definition of haqiqah: a word placed by language to signify a specific meaning. Understand that any use outside that meaning is majaz.
The etymology of haqiqah is given: from haqqa (to be established) or haqqaqtu (to establish).
2
Distinguish haqiqah from majaz
Note that haqiqah is the original meaning, while majaz requires a diverting clue (qarīna). A word is haqiqah for its primary meaning and majaz for secondary uses.
Example: 'asad' is haqiqah for lion, majaz for a brave man.
3
State the Hanafi claim
The passage asserts that haqiqah and majaz cannot be intended from the same word in one state. This is because a word cannot simultaneously be stable (haqiqah) and departing (majaz).
The claim is supported by linguistic practice: Arabs never use a word in both senses together.
4
Examine the opposing view
Imam al-Shafi'i and some Mu'tazila argue that combining is possible, citing the example 'do not marry what your father married' intending both intercourse and contract.
Their proof is psychological: one can intend both meanings simultaneously.
5
Analyze the Hanafi response
Hanafis reject this: the diverting clue is a condition for majaz, so intending literal meaning with the clue is contradictory. Also, a single thing cannot be both stable and departing.
The fiqh example of the wooden sa' shows that the hadith prohibition on riba applies only to haqiqah (silver coins), not to non-ribawi items.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الحقيقة والمجاز
كل لفظ وَضَعَه واضعُ اللغة بإزاء شيء فهو حقيقة له.
الحقيقة وهي إما فعيل بمعنى فاعل من حق الشيء إذا ثبت وإما بمعنى مفعول من حققت الشيء إذا أثبته، فيكون معناها الثابتة أو المثبتة في موضعها الأصلي، والتاء على هذا للنقل من الوصفية إلى الاسمية كذا وجدته في "مرأة الأصول"، وعند صاحب "المفتاح" للتأنيث. وإنما جمعهما في فصل واحد لابتناء المجاز على الحقيقة، أو لاشتراكهما في وجود ما أريد به خاصاً كان أو عاماً، أو لتحقق التقابل بينهما. [عمدة الحواشي: ص ٣٢] كل لفظ وَضَعَه إلخ والوضع تعيين اللفظ بإزاء المعنى بنفسه ليدل عليه بنفسه بلا واسطة قرينة كالأسد؛ فإنه للهيكل المخصوص بحيث يدل عليه عند الإطلاق بلا انضمام قرينة فكان حقيقة فيه، ولو استعمل في موضع الرجل الشجاع كان مجازا.
ولو استعمل في غيره يكون مجازاً لا حقيقةً. ثم الحقيقة مع المجاز لا يجتمعان إرادةً من لفظ واحد في حالة واحدة.
لا يرادان معا
ولهذا قلنا لما أريد ما يدخل في الصاع بقوله عليه: "لا تبيعوا الدرهم بالدرهمين،
لامتناع اجتماعهما
ولا الصاع بالصاعين
تلك
لا يجتمعان لأن أهل اللغة لم يستعملوا اللفظ الواحد في المعنى الحقيقي والمجازي معاً أصلاً، فكان استعماله فيهما خارجاً عن لغتهم، فلا يجوز، والشافعي محله ومن المعتزلة عبد الجبار والجبائي ذهبوا إلى أنهما يجتمعان، واستدلوا فيه بأن لا مانع من إرادة المعنيين المختلفين جميعاً، فإنا نجد في أنفسنا إرادة المعنيين من إرادة واحدة، ومن أنكر ذلك فقد أنكر البداهة ألا ترى أنه لو قال: لا تنكح ما نكح أبوك وأراد الوطء والعقد؛ فإنه صحيح من غير استحالة وقال أصحابنا إن القرينة الصارفة عن المعنى الحقيقي شرط للمجاز، فإرادة المعنى الحقيقي مع القرينة محال؛ لأنه خلف عنها والخلف لا يثبت إلا بعد فوت الأصل عقلاً، وأيضاً أن الحقيقة مستقرة في محله، والمجاز متجاوز عنه، والشيء الواحد يستحيل أن يستقر في محله ويتجاوز عنه في حالة واحدة، فاستحال الجميع كما استحال أن يكون الثوب الواحد على اللابس ملكاً وعارية كذا في "المعدن". ولهذا أي لأجل أنهما لا يجتمعان أجمعوا على أن نفس الصاع (وهو الخشبة المنقورة) جاز بيعه متفاضلا بجنسه؛ لعدم دخوله تحت النهي وهو قوله علي: "لا تبيعوا الدرهم بالدرهمين" الحديث.

لا تبيعوا إلخ: لم نجده بهذا اللفظ عن ابن عمر لما لكن روى الطحاوي عن عثمان له مرفوعاً: "لا تبيعوا الدينار بالدينارين، ولا الدرهم بالدرهمين" وروى هو والحاكم في مستدركه" عن ابن عمر ما "الدينار بالدينار والدرهم بالدرهم لا فضل بينهما" إلخ ثم الحديث المذكور أخرجه الزيلعي، وأخرج مسلم عن الخدري كنا نرزق تمراً الجمع فكنا نبيع رسول الصاعين بالصاع، فبلغ ذلك رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم فقال: "لا تبيعوا صاعي تمر بصاع، ولا صاعي حنطة بصاع، ولا درهماً بدرهمين، وروى ابن ماجه وغيره عنه بلفظ: "لا يباع صاع تمر بصاعين، ولا در هم بدر همين" كذا في "الفصول".
*
أخرجه أحمد في "مسنده" رقم: ۵۸۸۵، ۱۰۹/۲۲، وعبد الرزاق في "مصنفه" ۳۳/۸، ومجمع الزوائد ١١٣/٤ عن ابن عمر الله، وفيه أبو جناب وهو ثقة ولكنه مدلس.
Section on truth and metaphor. Every word that the language-putter placed to correspond to something, it is a truth for it. The truth is either a hyperbole pattern meaning 'established' from 'haqqa' or a passive meaning 'established' from 'haqqaqtu', so its meaning is 'the established' or 'the affirmed' in its original place, and the taa here is for transfer from adjective to noun. They are combined in one section because metaphor is based on truth, or because they share in existence of what is intended whether specific or general, or to realize the contrast between them. [from Umdat al-Hawashī] Every word ... and the placing is the assignment of the word to the meaning by itself to indicate it by itself without a clue, like 'asadu'; it is for the specific figure such that it indicates it upon absolute use without a clue, so it is truth in it, and if used in place of a brave man, it would be metaphor. And if used in other than it, it is metaphor, not truth. Then truth and metaphor are not combined intending from a single word in one state. They are not intended together. For this we said, when what enters the sa' was intended by his saying 'do not sell a dirham for two dirhams', due to impossibility of their combination, 'nor a sa' for two sa's'. They do not combine because the people of the language did not use a single word in both truth and metaphor together at all, so using it in both is outside their language, so it is not permissible. Al-Shafi'i and from the Mu'tazila Abd al-Jabbar and al-Juba'i held that they combine, arguing that there is no impediment to intending both different meanings, for we find in ourselves intending both meanings from one intention, and whoever denies that has denied intuition. Do you not see that if one said 'do not marry what your father married' intending both intercourse and contract, it is valid without impossibility. Our companions said that the clue diverting from truth is a condition for metaphor, so intending truth with the clue is impossible because it contradicts it, and contradiction only occurs after the original is lost rationally. Also, truth is stable in its place, and metaphor departs from it, and a single thing cannot simultaneously be stable and departing in one state, so all is impossible, just as a single garment cannot be both owned and borrowed. For this, they agreed that the sa' itself (the carved wood) is permissible to be sold with disparity of its kind, because it does not fall under the prohibition which is 'do not sell a dirham for two dirhams.' As for the hadith, we did not find it with this wording from Ibn Umar...
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage is a definition and discussion of haqiqah (literal truth) and majaz (metaphor) in usul al-fiqh. It begins by defining haqiqah as a word placed by language to signify a specific meaning, and if used for something else, it becomes majaz. The author then argues that haqiqah and majaz cannot be intended together from the same word in a single utterance. This is supported by linguistic evidence: Arabic speakers never use a word to convey both its literal and metaphorical meanings simultaneously. The passage then presents the opposing view of Imam al-Shafi'i and some Mu'tazila (Abd al-Jabbar and al-Juba'i) who argue that it is possible, using the example of the phrase 'do not marry what your father married' which could mean both intercourse and contract. The Hanafi response is that the diverting clue (qarīna) is a condition for majaz, so intending the literal meaning with the clue present is contradictory. Further, a single thing cannot be both stable (haqiqah) and departing (majaz) at the same time. An example from fiqh: the hadith prohibiting selling a dirham for two dirhams does not apply to the wooden sa' (a measure) because it is not a ribawi item. The footnote provides textual variants of the hadith.

Key Terms
الحقيقة (al-ḥaqīqah): A word placed by the language to indicate its specific meaning; the established meaning in its original place.
المجاز (al-majāz): Uses of a word in other than its original meaning; it requires a diverting clue (qarīna).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, why can a single word not convey both its literal and metaphorical meanings simultaneously?
A) Because the Arabic language does not allow it, as confirmed by consensus.
B) Because the diverting clue (qarīna) is a condition for majaz, and intending the literal meaning with the clue contradicts the purpose of the clue.
C) Because the majority of scholars agree on this.
D) Because the Prophet (PBUH) prohibited it.

Key Takeaway
Haqiqah and majaz are mutually exclusive for a single word in a single instance; the presence of a diverting clue makes the literal meaning impossible.

Haqiqah and Majaz Cannot Be Intended Together: The Hanafi Argument
Argument for impossibility of combining literal and metaphorical intent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that haqiqah and majaz cannot be intended together from a single word, and evaluate the opposing argument from al-Shafi'i and the Mu'tazila.

Core Idea
A word cannot mean both literal and metaphorical sense at once; only one is intended.

In this lesson, we examine a foundational rule of Hanafi usul: the impossibility of intending both literal (haqiqah) and metaphorical (majaz) meanings from a single word in one instance. This rule has far-reaching implications for interpreting legal texts, from the Quran and Hadith to contracts and bequests. We will explore the linguistic and rational arguments for this position and engage with the opposing view.

Guided Path
1
Define haqiqah and majaz
Identify the clear definitions from the text: haqiqah is the original meaning, majaz is a secondary meaning requiring a qarina (contextual indicator).
These terms are central to the discussion.
2
State the Hanafi rule
The rule: haqiqah and majaz cannot be intended together from a single word in one instance. They are mutually exclusive.
This is a definitive principle in Hanafi usul.
3
Identify the opposing argument
Al-Shafi'i and the Mu'tazila (e.g., 'Abd al-Jabbar, al-Juba'i) argue that there is no rational impediment to intending both meanings, citing the example of 'nikah' (marriage/intercourse).
The opposing view is based on intuition and perceived linguistic flexibility.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response: a qarina is necessary for majaz; intending the literal meaning alongside the qarina is contradictory. Also, a single word cannot be simultaneously stable (haqiqah) and transferred (majaz), like a garment cannot be both owned and borrowed.
This logical argument refutes the opposing claim.
5
Apply the rule to examples
Work through the examples in the text: the sa' measure, bequests to mawali and abkar, aman for fathers, and bequest to sons. See how the rule determines inclusion or exclusion.
These examples demonstrate practical application in fiqh rulings.

البحث الأول في كتاب الله
فصل في الحقيقة والمجاز
سَقَطَ اعتبار نفس الصاع حتى جاز بيعُ الواحد منه بالإثنين. ولما أُريد الوقاعُ من آية المُلامَسَة، سقط اعتبار إرادة المس باليد.
قال محمد الله: "إذا أوصى لمواليه، وله موال أعتَقَهم، والموالية موال أعتقوهم ، كانت للموصي الوصيّة لمواليه دون موال "مواليه، وفي "السير" الكبير": "لو استأمن أهل الحرب على آبائهم لا تدخلُ الأجداد في الأمان، ولو استأمنوا على أمهاتهم لا يثبت الأمان في حق الجدات".
وعلى هذا قلنا: إذا أوصى لأبكار بني فلان لاتدخلُ المُصَابةُ بالفجور في حكم الوصية.
الحقيقة والمجاز لا يجتمعان
=
ولو أوصى لبني فلان، وله بنون وبنُو بَنِيه كانت الوصية لبنيه دون بني بنيه.
آية الملامسة:
هذه ﴿وَإِنْ كُنتُمْ جُنُباً فَاطَّهَّرُوا وَإِنْ كُنتُمْ مَرْضَى أَوْ عَلَى سَفَرٍ أَوْ جَاءَ أَحَدٌ مِنْكُمْ مِنَ الْغَائط وهو أَوْ لامَسْتُمُ النِّسَاءَ فَلَمْ تَجِدُوا مَاءً فَتَيَمَّمُوا صَعِيداً طيبا الآية. لا تدخل إلخ: لأنهم ليسوا آباء حقيقة بل مجازاً بتوسط كونهم آباء للآباء، فإذا أريد به الأباء الحقيقية لا تراد الأجداد؛ لامتناع الجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز، وإنما تدخل أبناء الأبناء في أمان الأبناء لا لأن الأبناء يشملهم جمعاً، بل لأن ظاهر الاسم يشملهم عرفاً كما في بني أدم، وبني اسرائيل، وبني هاشم، فالشبهة بشمول ظاهر الاسم عرفاً كفت في حقن الدم وحفظه، فيدخلون تبعاً، وهو بالفروع أليق؛ إذ الأصل لا يتبع الفرع، وهو يتبعه، فلا تدخل الأجداد؛ لأنهم أصول الآباء. ولو استأمنوا : لأن اسم الأب والأم حقيقة في الوالد والوالدة، وإنما يقال للجد أب، وللجدة أم على سبيل المجاز، وقد أريد الآباء والأمهات في الاستثمان عليهم، فلا يكون الأجداد والجدات مرادة؛ لئلا يلزم الجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز. لا تدخلُ المُصابة إلخ لأن البكر حقيقة غير المصابة، وإنما سمي المصابة بالزنا بكراً؛ لأنها لم تزوج، وإنما قيدت بالفجور؛ لأنه لو زالت بوثبة أو حيضة أو تعنيس أو جراحة ونحوها تدخل في الوصية؛ لأنها بكر حقيقة. في حكم الوصية: لأن حقيقة البكر في المرأة ما في بطن فرجها من نسج، وقد انخرق بالزنا. دون بني بنيه: أي إذا أوصى أحد لأبناء زيد مثلاً، وله بنون وبنو بينه يدخل في الوصية الأبناء، ولا يدخل فيه أبناء الأبناء؛ لأن لفظ الابن حقيقة في الابن ومجاز في ابن الابن فلا يجتمع مع الحقيقة، وقالا : يدخل أبناء الأبناء أيضاً؛ لأن اللفظ يطلق عليهم، فيتناولهم باعتبار الظاهر كذا في "نور الأنوار".
The first research on the Book of Allah. Section on literal and metaphorical meaning. If it is used in other than its literal meaning, it is metaphorical, not literal. Moreover, literal and metaphorical meanings cannot both be intended from a single word in one instance. They are not intended together. For this reason, we say: when what enters into the sa' (a measure) was intended by his saying, peace be upon him: 'Do not sell a dirham for two dirhams' due to the impossibility of combining them, 'nor a sa' for two sa's'. They cannot be combined because the people of the language have never used one word for both literal and metaphorical meanings together originally, so using it for both would be outside their language, thus impermissible. Al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, and from the Mu'tazila 'Abd al-Jabbar and al-Juba'i, hold that they can be combined. They argue that there is no impediment to intending two different meanings together, for we find in ourselves the intention of two meanings from one intention, and whoever denies that denies intuition. Do you not see that if one says: 'Do not marry what your father married' intending both intercourse and the marriage contract, it is valid without impossibility? Our companions say: the contextual indicator (qarina) diverting from the literal meaning is a condition for metaphor, so intending the literal meaning along with the qarina is impossible because it contradicts the qarina, and contradiction can only occur after the original is rationally impossible. Also, the literal meaning is settled in its place, while the metaphorical is transferred from it, and one thing cannot be settled and transferred at the same time, so it is impossible, just as it is impossible for one garment worn by a person to be both owned and borrowed. This is as stated in al-Ma'din. For this reason, because they cannot be combined, they unanimously agreed that the sa' itself (which is a carved wooden measure) can be sold with disparity in exchange for its own kind, because it does not fall under the prohibition, which is his saying, peace be upon him: 'Do not sell a dirham for two dirhams' etc. || 'Do not sell' etc.: We did not find it with this wording from Ibn 'Umar, but al-Tahawi narrated from 'Uthman, may Allah be pleased with him, as raised (marfu'): 'Do not sell a dinar for two dinars, nor a dirham for two dirhams.' He and al-Hakim in his Mustadrak narrated from Ibn 'Umar: 'A dinar for a dinar, a dirham for a dirham, no excess between them' etc. Then the aforementioned hadith was extracted by al-Zayla'i, and Muslim narrated from al-Khudri: 'We used to receive mixed dates and we would sell two sa's of dates for one sa'. That reached the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, and he said: 'Do not sell two sa's of dates for one sa, nor two sa's of wheat for one sa, nor a dirham for two dirhams.' Ibn Majah and others narrated from him with the wording: 'A sa' of dates should not be sold for two sa's, nor a dirham for two dirhams.' This is as in al-Fusul. * It was extracted by Ahmad in his Musnad, number 5885, 22/109, and by 'Abd al-Razzaq in his Musannaf, 8/33, and Majma' al-Zawa'id 4/113 from Ibn 'Umar, may Allah be pleased with them both, and it contains Abu Janab, who is trustworthy but a mudallis (one who omits the link). [Page 35] The first research on the Book of Allah. Section on literal and metaphorical meaning. The consideration of the sa' itself fell away, so selling one of it for two became permissible. And when intercourse was intended from the verse of touching (mulamasa), the intention of touching with the hand fell away. Muhammad, may Allah be pleased with him, said: 'If he bequeaths to his mawali (freed slaves), and he has mawali whom he freed, and the mawali are those who freed them, the bequest is for his mawali, not for the mawali of his mawali.' And in al-Siyar al-Kabir: 'If the people of war seek safe conduct (aman) for their fathers, the grandfathers do not enter into the aman, and if they seek aman for their mothers, the aman is not established for the grandmothers.' And on this basis, we say: If he bequeaths to the virgins (abkar) of the sons of so-and-so, the one who has been deflowered by fornication does not enter into the ruling of the bequest. Literal and metaphorical meanings do not combine. = And if he bequeaths to the sons of so-and-so, and he has sons and grandsons, the bequest is for his sons, not for his grandsons. The verse of mulamasa: This is {And if you are in a state of junub, purify yourselves, and if you are sick or on a journey, or one of you comes from the toilet, or you have touched women, and you find no water, then perform tayammum with clean earth} the verse. 'They do not enter' etc.: because they are not fathers literally, but metaphorically through being fathers of fathers, so if literal fathers are intended, grandfathers are not intended, due to the impossibility of combining literal and metaphorical. Grandsons enter into the aman of sons, not because 'sons' includes them in a general sense, but because the apparent meaning of the term includes them by custom, as in 'children of Adam', 'children of Israel', and 'children of Hashim'. So the ambiguity of the apparent meaning of the term by custom sufficed for the protection of blood and its preservation, so they enter as followers, and this is more appropriate for branches, because the origin does not follow the branch, but the branch follows it. So grandfathers do not enter, because they are the origins of fathers. 'If they seek aman': because the term 'father' and 'mother' is literal for the parent, and the grandfather is called father and the grandmother mother only metaphorically, and the literal fathers and mothers were intended in seeking aman for them, so grandfathers and grandmothers are not intended, so as not to necessitate combining literal and metaphor. 'The deflowered one does not enter' etc. because the virgin (bikr) literally is the one not deflowered, and the one deflowered by fornication is called a virgin only because she has not been married. It is restricted to fornication because if the hymen is lost due to a jump, menstruation, spinsterhood, injury, or similar, she enters into the bequest, because she is a literal virgin. 'In the ruling of the bequest': because the literal meaning of 'bikr' for a woman is what is in her vagina from tissue, and it has been torn by fornication. 'Not for his grandsons': i.e., if someone bequeaths to the sons of Zayd, for example, and he has sons and grandsons, the sons enter the bequest, and the grandsons do not enter, because the word 'son' is literal for the son and metaphorical for the grandson, so it cannot combine with the literal. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say: grandsons also enter, because the word is applied to them and includes them by virtue of the apparent meaning, as in Nur al-Anwar.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage from al-Shashi's Usul addresses the fundamental rule that literal (haqiqah) and metaphorical (majaz) meanings cannot be simultaneously intended from a single word in a single instance. The author first defines haqiqah: using a word in its original meaning, and majaz: using it in another sense. He then states the rule that they cannot be intended together. He provides a proof: the people of language never used a single word for both meanings simultaneously, so using it that way would be outside their convention, hence invalid. Then he presents the opposing view of al-Shafi'i and Mu'tazila scholars like 'Abd al-Jabbar and al-Juba'i, who argue that there is no rational impediment to intending both meanings, citing the example of 'do not marry what your father married' where both intercourse and the contract can be intended. The Hanafi response is that a qarina (contextual indicator) diverting from the literal is necessary for majaz; intending the literal alongside the qarina is contradictory. Also, logically, one thing cannot be settled in its place (haqiqah) and transferred (majaz) simultaneously, just as a garment cannot be both owned and borrowed. The text then gives examples: the sa' measure in the hadith is a carved wooden container, not a standard measure, so it can be sold with disparity because the prohibition on riba applies to the measure, not the container. But when the literal meaning (the container) is intended, the metaphorical meaning (the standard measure) is not, and vice versa. Further examples from bequests and aman illustrate the rule: in bequests to 'mawali', only direct freed slaves are included, not their freed slaves, because 'mawali' is literal for direct freed slaves and metaphorical for the freed slaves of one's own freed slaves. Similarly, when seeking aman for 'fathers', grandfathers are not included because 'father' is literal for direct father and metaphorical for grandfather. In bequests to 'abkar' (virgins), a woman deflowered by fornication is not included because the literal meaning of 'bikr' is one whose hymen is intact, which is lost by fornication, but deflowering by other means (jump, menses, etc.) does not affect the literal meaning, so those women are included. Also, bequest to 'sons' does not include grandsons because 'son' is literal for direct son and metaphorical for grandson; however, the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that grandsons are included because the word is customarily applied to them, as in 'Bani Adam'. The text also references the verse of tayammum (5:6) with the word 'lamastum' meaning 'to touch'. In the hadith prohibition of riba, the intention is to mean the measurable commodity, not the physical container. The commentary is based solely on the Arabic text, and no external rulings are introduced.

Key Terms
الحقيقة (al-ḥaqīqah): Using a word in its original, established meaning as per linguistic convention.
المجاز (al-majāz): Using a word in a meaning other than its original, based on a qarina (contextual indicator) and a relationship (alāqah).
القرينة (al-qarīnah): A contextual indicator (verbal or situational) that shows the intended meaning is other than the literal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view presented in the passage, why does the woman deflowered by fornication not enter into a bequest for 'abkar' (virgins)?
A) Because she is no longer a virgin literally, as the literal meaning of 'bikr' requires an intact hymen, and it is torn by fornication.
B) Because she committed a sin and thus is excluded out of punishment.
C) Because the word 'bikr' only applies to those who have never been married, not to physical integrity.
D) Because fornication alters her legal status entirely.

Key Takeaway
The rule that literal and metaphorical meanings cannot be intended together is a cornerstone of Hanafi usul, preventing ambiguity in legal interpretation.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
12 min
The Impossibility of Combining Literal and Figurative: Hanafi Defense Against Objections
Hanafi refutation of objections against combining literal and figurative

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi argument that apparent combinations of haqiqah and majaz are actually pure majaz due to custom, and evaluate the opposing view.

Core Idea
Combining haqiqah and majaz is impossible; customary usage ensures that only one meaning is intended.

In this lesson, we examine a key usul dispute: can a single utterance simultaneously carry both literal and figurative meanings? The Hanafi school firmly denies this, and here we see a detailed defense against objections from those who claim that certain oath examples prove combination. Through analysis of common oaths, the commentator shows that custom (urf) causes the entire expression to be interpreted figuratively, preserving the principle.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi principle and the opposing objection
Recall the Hanafi rule: it is impossible to intend both haqiqah and majaz in one utterance. Then identify the objection: opponents point to oath examples where both meanings seem to be used.
The opponents are likely Shafi'i scholars who accept combination in certain cases.
2
Identify the three oath examples used by opponents
List the examples: (1) 'I will not place my foot in so-and-so's house' – breaking the oath if entering riding; (2) 'I will not reside in so-and-so's house' – applying to rented/borrowed houses; (3) 'My slave is free on the day so-and-so arrives' – breaking if arrival is at night.
Each example appears to mix literal and figurative meanings.
3
Follow the Hanafi response for each example
Understand that custom (urf) transforms the entire phrase: 'placing foot' becomes a metaphor for entering, 'house of so-and-so' means any dwelling he occupies, and 'day' means absolute time of arrival. Thus no combination occurs.
The response relies on the concept of 'common metaphor' (majaz muta'arif) and contextual evidence.
4
Understand the concept of 'general figurative meaning' (umum al-majaz)
Recognize that the Hanafis claim the whole expression is figurative, not part literal part figurative. This preserves the rule against combination.
This is distinct from combining both meanings.
5
Recognize the role of intention and custom in oath rulings
Note that if the swearer has a specific intention (e.g., barefoot only), that intention is honored. Otherwise, custom determines the metaphorical meaning.
This shows flexibility within the framework.

أي قوله : لا ينكح قال أصحابنا له : "لو حَلَفَ لا ينكح فلانة وهي أجنبيَّة، كان ذلك على العقد حتى لو زنى بها لا يحنَثُ. ولئن قال: إذا حلف لا يضَعُ قَدَمَهُ في دار فلان يحنَثُ لو دَخلَها لعدم إرادة الوطء بالنكاح حافياً أو متغلا أو راكباً. وكذلك لو حلف لا يسكن دار فلان يحنث لو كانت الدار ملكاً لفلان أو كانت بأجرة أو عارية"، وذلك جمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز. وكذلك لو قال: "عبده حرِّ يومَ يقدم فلان، فقدمَ فلانٌ ليلاً أو نهاراً يحنث". قلنا: وضعُ القدم صار مجازاً عن الدخول بحكم العرف، على العقد إلخ: قلت: هذا مناف لما سبق منه من حمل النكاح على الوطء في قوله تعالى: حَتَّى تَنْكِحَ زَوْجاً غَيْرَهُ ، ولما قدمنا في قوله تعالى: ﴿ومَا نَكَحَ آباءُ كُم حتى حرمنا مزنية الأب وأمته المؤطؤة أيضاً، ولذا أورد بعض الفضلاء بأن المقدم الحقيقة الشرعية على اللغوية لا العكس، ولا حاجة في الآية إلى حمله على الوطء، قلنا: هذا وإن كان مجازاً شرعياً وحقيقة لغوية فهو مجاز متعارف وهو كثير في النصوص شائع ومستفيض فيهما كما لا يخفى على الفاحص المتفطن، فيمكن إرادته عند جواز إرادة العقد أيضاً كذا في "الفصول". ولئن إلخ: لما فرغ عن بيان الأصل وهو: أن الجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز مستحيل شرع في بيان النقوض الواردة على هذا الأصل. إذا حلف إلخ حاصل السوال وجود الجمع بينهما عندكم في هذه الأمثلة؛ لأن حقيقة وضع القدم في دخولها حافياً لا راكباً، وحقيقة إضافة الدار إلى فلان كونها ملكاً له، وحاصل الجواب: أنه ليس جمعاً بينهما بل هو عموم المجاز؛ لقرينة العرف القاضي على اللغة فافهم كذا في "الفصول". يحنث لو دَخلَها إلخ: هذا إذا لم يكن له نية، ولو نوى حين حلف أن لا يضع قدمه فيها حافياً فدخلها راكباً لم يحنث، ويصدق ديانة وقضاء؛ لأنه نوى حقيقة كلامه، وهذه حقيقة مستعملة غير مهجورة. دار فلان وللإضافة إلى فلان بالملك حقيقة؛ لأنها بمعنى اللام وهي للملك، وبغيره مجاز؛ لصحة النفي وهو من أمارات المجاز. وذلك جمع إلخ: أي كون الحنث في الدخول حافياً أو راكباً، وكون الدار ملكاً أو عارية أو إجارة. يحنت وهذا أيضاً جمع بينهما؛ لأن اليوم للنهار حقيقة، والليل مجاز . بحكم العرف إلخ: أي بطريق إطلاق اسم السبب على المسبب؛ لأن وضع القدم سبب للدخول، وإنما تركت حقيقة بدلالة العرف والعادة؛ لأن الحالف الامتناع عن الدخول لا عن نفس وضع القدم، فكأنه قال: لا أدخل دار فلان. والدخولُ لا يتفاوَتُ في الفصلين، ودار فلان صار مجازاً عن دار مسكونة له، وذلك لا يتفاوت بين أن يكون ملكاً له، أو كانت بأجرة له، واليوم في مسألة القدوم عبارة عن مطلق الوقت؛ لأن اليوم إذا أُضيف إلى فعل لا يُمتَدُّ يكون عبارة عن مطلق الوقت من غير تقييد بالامتداد كما عُرفَ، فكان الحنْتُ بهذا الطريق، لا بطريق الجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز. من غير تقييد لا يتفاوت إلخ: سواء كان يوجد حافياً ومتنعلاً أو راكباً، فيعم الحنث بعموم المجاز لا بطريق الجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز. عن دار مسكونة إلخ ويمكن الجواب أيضاً بأن الإضافة لا يستلزم الملك بل مطلق التعلق والملابسة، وفي أمثال الدار والكتاب وغيرها مطلق القبض كما يقال: بيت عائشة وغير ذلك كذا في "الفصول". الوقت: وهو الجزء من الزمان ليلاً كان أو نهاراً؛ لأن ذكر اليوم لظرفية الفعل المقترن به، فإذا كان غير ممتد كالدخول والخروج يكفي له نفس الظرف وهو مطلق الوقت والوقت كما يطلق على النهار يطلق على الليل. عن مطلق الوقت: بخلاف ما إذا نسب إلى فعل ممتد كاللبس والركوب ونحوهما، فإنه يقال: لبست يوماً أو يومين، وركبت يوماً أو يومين فحيئذ، يكون اليوم عبارة عن بياض النهار ؛ رعاية للتناسب بين الظرف والمظروف، فكما أن الركوب واللبس فيهما امتداد كذلك في النهار امتداد.
Meaning of his statement: 'He does not marry.' Our companions said to him: 'If he swore not to marry a certain woman who is a stranger, that applies to the contract, so even if he commits zina with her, he does not break his oath. And if he said: if he swears not to place his foot in the house of so-and-so, he breaks his oath if he enters it even if barefoot, wearing sandals, or riding. Similarly, if he swears not to reside in the house of so-and-so, he breaks his oath if the house is owned by so-and-so or is rented or borrowed.' That is combining literal and figurative. Similarly, if he said: 'His slave is free on the day so-and-so arrives,' and so-and-so arrives at night or day, he breaks his oath. We said: Placing the foot has become a metaphor for entering by custom. As for the contract, etc.: I said: This contradicts what was stated earlier about interpreting marriage as intercourse in the verse 'until she marries another husband' and in what we presented in the verse 'and marry not women whom your fathers married'? and so on. Some virtuous scholars raised the point that precedence is given to the legal literal meaning over the linguistic, not the opposite, and there is no need in the verse to interpret it as intercourse. We said: Although this is a legal metaphor and a linguistic literal meaning, it is a common metaphor which is frequent in texts, common and widespread, as is not hidden from a careful examiner. So it can be intended when intending the contract is also permissible, as in 'al-Fusul'. And the statement 'and if he said ... etc.': When he finished explaining the principle that combining literal and figurative is impossible, he began explaining the objections raised against this principle. The summary of the objection: There is a combination of both in these examples according to you, because the literal meaning of placing the foot is entering barefoot, not riding, and the literal meaning of adding the house to so-and-so is ownership. The summary of the answer: It is not a combination but rather a general application of the metaphor due to the contextual evidence of custom overriding language. Understand this, as in 'al-Fusul'. 'He breaks his oath if he enters it etc.': This is if he has no intention. If when swearing he intended not to place his foot in it barefoot, and then he entered riding, he does not break his oath, and he is believed in private and in court because he intended the literal meaning of his words, and this literal meaning is used and not abandoned. 'The house of so-and-so' and the addition to so-and-so by ownership is literal because it is like the letter 'lam' which indicates ownership, otherwise it is metaphorical because negation is valid, which is a sign of metaphor. 'That is combining etc.': Meaning that breaking the oath upon entering barefoot or riding, and the house being owned, borrowed, or rented. 'He breaks his oath' and this is also combining because 'day' literally means daytime and 'night' is metaphorical. 'By custom etc.': Meaning by way of naming the cause for the effect, because placing the foot is a cause for entering, and the literal meaning was left due to custom and habit because the person swearing intends to avoid entering, not the actual placing of the foot, so it is as if he said: I will not enter the house of so-and-so. And entry does not differ in the two cases, and 'the house of so-and-so' has become a metaphor for a house inhabited by him, and that does not differ whether it is owned by him or rented. And 'the day' in the matter of arrival refers to absolute time, because when 'day' is added to an action that has no extension, it refers to absolute time without restriction of extension, as is known. Thus the breaking of the oath is through this way, not through combining literal and figurative. 'Without restriction' etc.: Meaning whether one enters barefoot, wearing sandals, or riding, so the breaking of the oath is general by the general application of the metaphor, not by combining literal and figurative. 'About a house inhabited etc.': Another possible answer is that addition does not necessitate ownership but rather absolute connection and relation, and in examples like house and book and others, it means absolute possession, as it is said: 'the house of Aisha' and so on, as in 'al-Fusul'. 'Time': It is the part of time, night or day, because mentioning 'day' is for the circumstance of the action associated with it. So when the action is non-extendable like entering and exiting, the circumstance itself is sufficient, which is absolute time. And 'time' is applied to night just as it is applied to daytime. 'About absolute time': In contrast to when it is related to an extendable action like wearing and riding and similar, for then it is said: I wore a day or two, I rode a day or two. In that case, 'day' refers to the brightness of daytime, considering the proportionality between the circumstance and the object, just as riding and wearing have extension, so too does the daytime have extension.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage is a usul dispute about the principle that combining literal (haqiqah) and figurative (majaz) meanings in a single utterance is impossible. The author (commentator on the matn) begins by quoting an objection: opponents point to examples from oaths where Hanafis supposedly combine both. For instance, if someone swears not to 'place his foot' in a house, he breaks the oath if he enters riding, even though 'placing the foot' literally means walking. Similarly, if he swears not to 'reside' in someone's house, he breaks the oath even if the house is rented, not owned. And if he swears to free a slave on 'the day' someone arrives, he breaks the oath if they arrive at night. The Hanafi response, explained in detail, is that in all these examples there is no combination; rather, the entire expression is interpreted figuratively due to custom (urf). 'Placing the foot' has become a common metaphor for 'entering' in general. 'The house of so-and-so' is commonly used for any dwelling he occupies, regardless of ownership. 'Day' when added to a punctual event like arrival means 'the time of arrival', whether day or night. The commentator defends this by noting that even when a term has both a literal and a metaphorical meaning, if the metaphor is widespread and customary, it can be intended. The internal Hanafi distinction between 'hafyan' (barefoot) and 'rakiban' (riding) is also discussed: if the swearer had a specific intention, that affects the ruling. The passage thus vindicates the Hanafi rule that haqiqah and majaz cannot coexist in one usage by showing that these examples are all cases of pure majaz.

Key Terms
الجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز (al-jam' bayn al-haqiqah wa al-majaz): The simultaneous use of both the literal and figurative meanings of a word in a single context.
حقيقة (haqiqah): Literally: the original meaning of a word as established by language or law.
مجاز (majaz): Figurative; a word used in a meaning other than its original due to a relation and a contextual indicator.
عرف (urf): Custom; prevalent usage that determines the meaning of words in context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi response in the lesson, why does the oath 'I will not place my foot in so-and-so's house' apply to riding?
A) Because 'placing foot' literally includes riding.
B) Because custom has turned 'placing foot' into a metaphor for entering, regardless of mode.
C) Because the swearer intended it that way.
D) Because the opponent's argument forced a combined meaning.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school maintains that combining literal and figurative in one utterance is impossible; apparent examples are resolved by interpreting the entire expression figuratively based on custom.

Three Types of Haqiqah: Muta'adhdhirah, Mahjurah, and Musta'malah
Classification of haqiqah into three types based on usage frequency

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three types of haqiqah and explain the Hanafi dispute when the literal is rare and the figurative is common.

Core Idea
A literal meaning can be abandoned or difficult; even when used rarely, its priority over common figurative is disputed among Hanafi imams.

This passage from Usul al-Shashi categorizes literal meanings (haqiqah) based on usage frequency. Understanding this classification is essential for interpreting texts where both literal and figurative meanings exist. The passage also presents an intra-Hanafi disagreement on how to resolve such conflicts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of haqiqah
Read the passage and list the three types: muta'adhdhirah (difficult/obsolete), mahjurah (abandoned), and musta'malah (used). Understand the defining trait of each.
Memorize the Arabic terms and their rough meanings.
2
Analyze the criterion for classification
The passage explains that the division is based on whether the literal meaning is actually used in common speech. For musta'malah, further subdivide into rare (nadirah) and common (sha'i'ah).
The key is customary usage (al-urf).
3
Recognize the Hanafi dispute
When the literal is rare and the figurative is common, Abu Hanifa holds the literal should be preferred; Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold the figurative. Understand the thamarah (practical consequence) for interpreting oaths or legal texts.
The text uses 'عنده' (him) for Abu Hanifa and 'عندهما' (them both) for the two companions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الحقيقة أنواع ثلاثة: متعذرة ومهجورة ومستعملة. ثم الحقيقة أنواع إلخ وجه الحصر: أن المعنى الحقيقي إما أن لا يكون مستعملاً أي: لم تجر العادة الفاشية لعامة أهل العرف والاستعمال باستعماله في نفسه، فصار ذلك منشأ لترك إرادته من لفظه كأكل الشجرة، أو باستعماله لفظه لا في نفسه كحلف وضع القدم، وكل منهما إما مطلقاً عموماً أو في خصوص مادة الكلام كما في الحلف حيث يراد الحقيقة في غير مادته كما في الإثبات أو يكون مستعملاً، فعلى الثاني الحقيقة مستعملة، وعلى الأول إما أن يمتنع إرادة الحقيقي امتناعاً عادياً بالتعذر أو التعسر إما في نفسه أو في الإرادة من اللفظ عموماً أو خصوصاً في مادة الكلام، أو لا يمتنع ولا يقع بالنظر إلى العرف بالفعل، فعلى الأول متعذرة، وعلى الثاني مهجورة، ثم المستعملة إما نادرة الاستعمال قليلة والمجاز شائعة وفاشية فالمجاز هو الراجح المتعين للإرادة في المتعذرة والمهجورة اتفاقاً، والحقيقة هي المقدمة المتعينة عند كونها مستعملة استعمالاً فاشياً اتفاقاً، وإنما الخلاف في النادرة الشاذة مع كون المجاز متعارفاً، فعنده الحقيقة أولى وعندهما المجاز أولى. متعذرة: هي ما لا يتوصل إليها إلا بمشقة كأكل الشجرة في المثال الآتي.
Then literal meaning is of three types: difficult (muta'adhdhirah), abandoned (mahjurah), and used (musta'malah). Then: 'Literal meaning is of types etc.' The justification for the enumeration is that the literal meaning either is not used—i.e., the widespread custom of the people of custom and usage does not use it in itself, so that becomes the reason for abandoning intending it from its word, like 'eating the tree'—or it is used with its word but not in itself, like the oath of placing the foot. Each of these two is either absolutely general or specific to the context of speech, as in the oath where the literal is intended in other than its context, as in positive statements, or it is used. So on the second, the literal is used. On the first, either intending the literal is prevented by customary impossibility due to difficulty or hardship either in itself or in intending from the word generally or specifically in the context of speech, or it is not prevented and does not occur in view of custom actually. So on the first, it is difficult; on the second, abandoned. Then the used literal is either rare in usage, scanty, and the figurative is common and widespread. So the figurative is the preferred and determined for intention in the difficult and abandoned by agreement. And the literal is the preferred and determined when it is used in widespread usage by agreement. The only dispute is in the rare, anomalous literal with the figurative being common. According to him (Abu Hanifa?), the literal is better; according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad?), the figurative is better. 'Difficult' is that which cannot be reached except with difficulty, like 'eating the tree' in the coming example.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of haqiqah (literal meaning) from the widely-studied matn of Usul al-Shashi. The author identifies three types: muta'adhdhirah (difficult to use), mahjurah (abandoned), and musta'malah (used). The reasoning: a literal meaning may be completely unused in common speech (like 'eating the tree' as an example of something impossible to intend literally), or it may be used but not as the primary meaning (like in the oath 'placing foot' where the literal act of placing is not intended). The used type is further divided into rare (nadirah) and common (sha'i'ah). In the case of rare literal usage alongside common figurative, there is a well-known dispute within the Hanafi school: Abu Hanifa (referred to as 'him') prefers the literal, while the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, 'them') prefer the figurative. This classification is crucial for ijtihad and understanding how to interpret texts when multiple meanings are possible.

Key Terms
الحقيقة المتعذرة (al-haqiqah al-muta'adhdhirah): The literal meaning that is difficult to realize or understand without hardship, e.g., 'eating the tree' literally.
الحقيقة المهجورة (al-haqiqah al-mahjurah): The literal meaning that has been abandoned in common usage, not used at all.
الحقيقة المستعملة (al-haqiqah al-musta'malah): The literal meaning that is actually used in speech, either frequently or rarely.
المجاز المتعارف (al-majaz al-muta'āraf): The figurative meaning that is common and well-known in customary usage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Both agree that the figurative meaning is preferred.
B) Both agree that the literal meaning is preferred.
C) Abu Hanifa prefers the literal, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad prefer the figurative.
D) There is no ruling; it depends on the context.

Key Takeaway
The threefold classification of haqiqah (difficult, abandoned, used) combined with the intra-Hanafi dispute on rare literal vs. common figurative is essential for correct textual interpretation.

Three Types of Haqiqah: The Rule for Resorting to Majaz
Defines three types of haqiqah and the rule for resorting to majaz

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify haqiqah into its three types and explain the rule for resorting to majaz when the literal meaning is impossible or abandoned by custom.

Core Idea
When literal meaning is impossible or abandoned, consensus directs us to figurative meaning.

The distinction between haqiqah (literal) and majaz (figurative) is not merely theoretical—it determines the legal effect of oaths and other texts. This passage from Usul al-Shashi defines three types of haqiqah and establishes a key rule: where custom cannot apply the literal meaning, the figurative meaning takes over by consensus.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the three types
Identify the three types of haqiqah: mut'adhara (impossible), mahjura (abandoned), and musta'mala (commonly used). Understand that the first two require resorting to majaz, while the third retains haqiqah.
The terms are derived from 'usr (difficulty), hajr (abandonment), and 'amal (usage).
2
Understand the rule for mut'adhara and mahjura
Learn that by consensus (bi al-ittifaq), when the literal meaning is impossible (mut'adhara) or abandoned (mahjura), the word is taken figuratively (majaz). This rule applies in oaths and other contexts.
Impossibility here is customary, not rational.
3
Apply the rule to examples
Work through the examples: swearing not to eat from a tree/pot (oath refers to fruit/contents) and not to drink from a well (oath refers to drawing water). For the abandoned type, swearing not to place foot in a house (oath refers to entering).
Custom determines the figurative meaning.
4
Note the consensus
Recognize that the ruling for mut'adhara and mahjura is unanimous among Hanafis (by consensus). This strengthens the authority of the principle.
The third type (musta'mala) retains haqiqah, but this is not elaborated here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الحقيقة أنواع ثلاثة: متعذرة ومهجورة ومستعملة. وفي القسمين الأولين يُصَارُ إلى المجاز بالاتفاق. ونظير المتعذرة: إذا حلف لا يأكُلُ من هذه الشجرة، أو هذه القدر، فإن أكل الشجرة والقدر متعدّر أي عينها عادة ولو أمكن عقلا فينصرف ذلك إلى ثمرة الشجرة وإلى ما يحلُّ في القدر حتى لو أكل من عين الشجرة، أو من عين القدر بنوع تكلُّف لا يحنث. وعلى هذا قلنا: إذا حَلَفَ لا يَشْرَبُ من هذه البئر ينصرف ذلك إلى الاغتراف حتى لو فرضنا أنه لو كرّعَ بنوع تكلف لا يحنث بالاتفاق. ونظيرُ المهجورة: لو حلف لا يَضَعُ قدمه في دار فلان، فإن إرادة وضع القدم مهجورة عادة. وعلى هذا قلنا: التوكيل بنفس الخصومة ينصَرِفُ إلى مطلق جواب الخصم حتى يسع عن قيد الرد والاعتراف
Then haqiqah is of three types: impossible (mut‘adhara), abandoned (mahjura), and commonly used (musta‘mala). In the first two types, recourse is made to majaz by consensus. The example of the impossible: if one swears not to eat from this tree or this pot, then eating the tree or the pot itself is impossible—i.e., its actual substance customarily, even if possible rationally—so the oath is directed to the fruit of the tree and to what is cooked in the pot, such that if he ate the actual tree or the actual pot with some difficulty, he does not break the oath. Similarly, we ruled: if one swears not to drink from this well, the oath is directed to drawing water, such that even if we assume he drinks directly with difficulty, he does not break the oath by consensus. The example of the abandoned: if one swears not to place his foot in so-and-so's house, then intending the literal placing of the foot is abandoned customarily. Similarly, we ruled: the agency in litigation itself is directed to the absolute answering of the opponent, encompassing both denial and admission.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of haqiqah (literal meaning) into three types. The first type is mut‘adhara: the literal meaning is impossible to apply customarily, even if rationally possible. In oaths, the word is taken figuratively (majaz). The second type is mahjura: the literal meaning is possible but abandoned by customary usage; again majaz is used. The third type is musta‘mala: the literal meaning is commonly used; here haqiqah is preferred. The passage gives examples: for mut‘adhara, swearing not to eat from a tree or pot—the oath applies to the fruit or contents, not the physical object. Similarly for drinking from a well—the oath applies to drawing water, not drinking directly. For mahjura, swearing not to place foot in a house—the oath applies to entering, not literally placing the foot. Also, agency in litigation is interpreted broadly as any response, not limited to denial or admission. This demonstrates the Hanafi usul principle that when literal meaning is impossible or abandoned, the figurative meaning is taken by consensus.

Key Terms
متعذرة (mut'adhara): Literal meaning is impossible to apply customarily (e.g., eating a tree)
مهجورة (mahjura): Literal meaning is possible but abandoned by custom (e.g., placing foot in a house)
مستعملة (musta'mala): Literal meaning is commonly used and preferred

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, because drinking literally happened.
B) No, because the oath is interpreted as drawing water (majaz), not direct drinking.
C) It depends on the size of the well.
D) Only if he used a cup.

Key Takeaway
The classification of haqiqah into mut'adhara, mahjura, and musta'mala establishes that custom (urf) determines whether literal or figurative meaning applies in legal contexts.

Haqiqah and Majaz: Rulings and Disputes
Presents rulings on haqiqah and majaz with examples from oaths and agency

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi rules for preferring haqiqah over majaz, identify when majaz becomes necessary due to mahjoor, and apply the dispute to practical cases.

Core Idea
Haqiqah is default; when abandoned, majaz is needed; when both are in use, Abu Hanifa and Sahibayn differ on the criterion.

This passage from Usul al-Shashi lays down the fundamental rules for when to use the literal meaning (haqiqah) and when to resort to figurative meaning (majaz). It covers the exception when the literal meaning is prohibited (mahjoor), and the intra-Hanafi dispute on the criterion for common figurative usage. Understanding these principles is key to correctly deriving rulings from texts and oaths.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two scenarios
Identify when haqiqah is in use and when it is abandoned (mahjoor). The text first discusses mahjoor with the wakeel example, then moves to the case where haqiqah is in use.
Mahjoor can be by Shariah or custom.
2
Understand the default rule
When haqiqah is in use and there is no common majaz, all agree that haqiqah is preferred. This is the baseline.
3
Analyze the dispute when common majaz exists
Compare Abu Hanifa's position (preferring haqiqah) with the Sahibayn's position (preferring the generality of majaz). Understand their proofs.
The proof for Abu Hanifa is the principle that origin is haqiqah; for Sahibayn, majaz is more comprehensive.
4
Apply the dispute to the examples
Work through the oath examples: wheat, Euphrates, and meat. See how each position applies and note the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf).
The definition of 'majaz muta'arif' differs between the two views: tafahum vs ta'amul.
5
Examine the theoretical underpinning
Understand that Abu Hanifa considers majaz a substitute in wording, while the Sahibayn consider it a substitute in ruling. This explains the difference in approach.
This is a deeper theoretical point that reinforces the practical disagreement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الحقيقة والمجاز للوكيل أن يجيب بـ"نعم" كما يَسَعُه أن يُجيب بـ "لا"؛ لأن التوكيل بنفس الخصومة خصم مؤكله مهجور شرعاً وعادةً. ولو كانت الحقيقة مستعملة فإن لم يكن لها مجاز متعارف، فالحقيقة أولى صغيرنا"، وبهذا تكون حقيقة الذات المقيدة بصفة الصبا مهجورة شرعاً، فيصار إلى المجاز، وهو مطلق الذات فيحنث، وأما عادة: فلأن المهجور شرعاً مهجورة عادة ولذا ينصرف التوكيل إلى مطلق الجواب بالإقرار أو الإنكار مجازاً؛ لهجران الحقيقة [الشافي: ص ٧٠] مجاز متعارف : أي أغلب وأكثر استعمالاً في التفاهم بل كانت الحقيقة والمجاز كلاهما مستعملين على السواء، أو كانت الحقيقة أكثر استعمالاً من المجاز، فالحقيقة مشايخ ماوراء النهر: إن ما قاله مشايخ العراق قول أبي حنيفة لله، وما قاله مشايخ بلخ ،قولهما، بدليل ما إذا حلف لا يأكل لحماً فأكل لحم الآدمي أو الخنزير يحنث عنده؛ لأن التفاهم يقع عينه؛ فإنه يسمى لحماً، ولا يحنث عندهما؛ لأن التعامل لا يقع عليهما؛ لأن لحمهما لا يؤكل عادة. أبي حنيفة: لأن العمل بالأصل ممكن فلا يصار إلى الخلف عند وجوده إلا بدليل. أولى: لأن المقصود هو المعنى، والمعنى المجازي ههنا أرجح؛ لأنه اشتمل لدخول حكم الحقيقة تحت عمومه، وكان أولى. إلى عينها: فإن حقيقته الحاصل منها لا يحنث عنده وعندهما ينصرف إلى ما تتضمنه الحنطة بطريق عموم المجاز، فيحنث بأكلها، وبأكل الخبز الحاصل منها. وكذا لو حَلَفَ "لا يشرب من "الفُرات" ينصرف إلى الشرب منها كرعاً وعندهما إلى المجاز المتعارف وهو شُرب مائها بأي طريق كان. ثم المجاز عند أبي حنيفة محله خلف عن الحقيقة في حق اللفظ، وعندهما: خلف عن حكم الحقيقة كما يثبت الحقيقة بناء علـ صحة التكلم وعندهما هذا أسد للشجاع خلف في إثبات الشجاعة عن قوله: هذا أسد للهيكل في إثبات الأسدية، هذا هو المراد من خلفية حكم المجاز عن حكم الحقيقة عندهما، لما تقرر أنهما من أوصاف اللفظ لا أوصاف المعنى اتفاقاً، وليس المراد أن شجاعة الشجاع خلف عن أسدية الهيكل هذا ما اختاره المحققون في تفسير الخلفية على القولين لهما أن الحكم هو المقصود لا نفس العبارة، واعتبار الخلفية والإصالة فيما هو المقصود أولى من اعتباره في غيره، ولأبي حنيفة رحاله: أن الحقيقة والمجاز من جنس الألفاظ بإجماع أهل اللغة، فكان =
Chapter on Haqiqah and Majaz: The agent may answer with 'yes' just as he may answer with 'no'; because the appointment as agent for the litigation itself is, for the opponent of his principal, abandoned (mahjoor) by law and custom. If the literal meaning is used, and if it does not have a common figurative meaning, then the literal is preferred without dispute. If it has a common figurative meaning, then the literal is preferred according to Abu Hanifa, while according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), acting on the generality of the figurative is preferred. Example: If one swore 'I will not eat from this wheat', this refers to its substance according to him, so that if he ate from the wheat... And that which is abandoned by law is like that abandoned by custom; because the apparent state of Muslims is that they refrain from what is abandoned by law due to their religion and reason. Thus the appointment as agent for the litigation itself is directed to the absolute answer containing denial and confession, so that if he confessed something against his principal, it is valid, contrary to al-Shafi'i and Zufar (may Allah have mercy on them) from our companions. He may answer with 'no', i.e., denying what the opponent claimed, for the reality of litigation, which is denial - whether the claimant is truthful or false - is unlawful by law, abandoned by law and custom. As for law: due to his saying, peace be upon him: 'He is not of us who does not have mercy on our little ones.' And with this, the reality of the essence qualified by the attribute of youth is abandoned by law, so one resorts to the figurative, which is the absolute essence, so he breaks the oath. As for custom: because what is abandoned by law is abandoned by custom. Therefore, the appointment as agent is directed to the absolute answer with confession or denial figuratively, due to the abandonment of the literal. [al-Shafi p.70] Common figurative: i.e., more frequent and more used in communication. Rather, if both literal and figurative are used equally, or the literal is used more than the figurative, then the literal is preferred; because the origin in speech is the literal, and there is nothing to contradict it, so it is necessary to act upon it without dispute. They differed in the interpretation of 'common figurative': The scholars of Balkh said: It means common by usage/transaction. The scholars of Iraq said: It means common by communication. The scholars of Transoxiana said: What the scholars of Iraq said is the view of Abu Hanifa, and what the scholars of Balkh said is the view of them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). The proof is the case where one swore 'I will not eat meat' and then ate human or pig meat: he breaks the oath according to him (Abu Hanifa) because communication applies to it literally, for it is called meat; but he does not break the oath according to them because usage/transaction does not apply to them, as their meat is not eaten customarily. Abu Hanifa: because acting upon the origin is possible, so one does not resort to the substitute in its presence except with evidence. 'Preferred': because the intended meaning is the meaning, and the figurative meaning here is more preponderant because it includes the ruling of the literal under its generality, so it is preferred. 'To its substance': that its reality is the yield from it; he does not break the oath according to him, but according to them it refers to what the wheat contains by way of generality of the figurative, so he breaks the oath by eating it and by eating the bread produced from it. Similarly, if one swore 'I will not drink from the Euphrates', it refers to drinking from it by lapping according to him, and according to them to the common figurative, i.e., drinking its water by any means. Then, according to Abu Hanifa, the figurative is a substitute for the literal in respect to the wording, while according to them it is a substitute for the ruling of the literal, so the ruling is established. Its clarification: According to Abu Hanifa, one's utterance 'this is a lion' for the brave is a substitute for the utterance 'this is a lion' for the known carcass, without consideration of the substitution being in the ruling (i.e., bravery). Then the ruling is established based on the validity of the utterance, just as the literal is established based on the validity of the utterance. According to them, 'this is a lion' for the brave is a substitute in establishing bravery for the statement 'this is a lion' for the carcass in establishing lion-ness. This is the intended meaning of the substitution of the ruling of the figurative for the ruling of the literal according to them, since it is established that both (literal and figurative) are attributes of the wording, not attributes of the meaning, by agreement. It is not intended that the bravery of the brave is a substitute for the lion-ness of the carcass. This is what the verifiers chose in explaining the substitution on the two views. According to them, the ruling is the intended purpose, not the phrase itself, and considering substitution and originality in what is intended is more appropriate than considering it in other things. According to Abu Hanifa, literal and figurative are of the category of words by consensus of the people of language, so they are...
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage is from the chapter on Haqiqah and Majaz in Usul al-Fiqh. It begins by discussing the case of a wakeel (agent) appointed for litigation. The agent may answer either 'yes' (affirming the claim) or 'no' (denying). The reason given is that the literal meaning of litigation (which is denial of the claim) is considered 'mahjoor' (abandoned) both by Shariah and custom. Since it is prohibited to deny a rightful claim (based on the hadith 'He is not of us who does not have mercy on our little ones'), the literal meaning is abandoned, and the figurative meaning (a general answer of either admission or denial) is used. The passage then states a general rule: if the literal meaning is in use and there is no commonly recognized figurative meaning, the literal is preferred without disagreement. If there is a common figurative meaning, then Abu Hanifa prefers the literal, while the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) prefer acting on the generality of the figurative meaning. Examples follow: an oath not to eat specific wheat — Abu Hanifa says it refers only to the wheat grains themselves, while the companions say it includes any product of the wheat due to the general figurative use. Similarly, an oath not to drink from the Euphrates — Abu Hanifa restricts it to lapping directly from the river, while the companions include drinking its water by any method. The passage then delves into the nature of majaz as a substitute (khalf) for haqiqah. According to Abu Hanifa, majaz is a substitute in the wording (the lafz) itself, meaning that one uses the word figuratively in place of the literal word. According to the companions, majaz is a substitute in the ruling (hukm) — the figurative word is used to establish a ruling that is analogous to the literal ruling, and the substitution is in the effect (the bravery) versus the original attribute (lion-ness). The scholars of Balkh, Iraq, and Transoxiana differed on the interpretation of 'common figurative' (majaz muta'arif). The preferred view among Hanafis is that given by the scholars of Iraq (associated with Abu Hanifa) and the scholars of Balkh (associated with the two companions). The passage explains their positions with the example of eating human or pig meat in an oath: Abu Hanifa considers it a breach because in common speech it is called meat, while the companions do not because it is not customarily eaten.

Key Terms
الحقيقة (al-haqiqah): Usage of a word in its original, conventionally established meaning for which it was coined.
المجاز (al-majaz): Usage of a word in a meaning other than its original, due to a relationship (al-'alaqah) between the literal and figurative meanings, with a contextual indicator (qarinah) preventing the literal meaning.
مهجور (mahjoor): Abandoned or prohibited by Shariah or custom, such that the literal meaning cannot be used.
مجاز متعارف (majaz muta'arif): A figurative meaning that is commonly understood and frequently used in everyday speech and transactions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling if a person swears 'I will not eat meat' and then eats human meat?
A) The oath is broken according to Abu Hanifa, but not according to the Sahibayn.
B) The oath is not broken according to both.
C) The oath is broken according to both.
D) The ruling depends on the type of meat.

Key Takeaway
When haqiqah is in use and a common figurative meaning exists, Abu Hanifa prefers the literal, while the Sahibayn prefer the generality of the figurative; the practical difference appears in oaths and agency.

The Nature of Majaz: Substitution in Wording or Ruling?
Usul dispute on the nature of majaz and khalaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two Hanafi positions on the nature of majaz, apply the concept of khalaf (substitution), and evaluate the practical consequence (thamarah) of the disagreement.

Core Idea
Majaz as substitution: does it replace the wording or the ruling? The answer determines whether impossible literal meanings still yield metaphorical rulings.

This lesson delves into a classic usul dispute within the Hanafi school: Is majaz (metaphorical usage) a substitution in the wording (lafz) or in the ruling (hukm)? The answer, debated between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two senior companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani), has direct implications for how we interpret statements whose literal meaning is impossible. By examining examples from oaths and manumission, you will understand the theoretical underpinnings and practical outcomes of each view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
From the passage, extract Abu Hanifa's view that majaz is khalaf in the wording, and the Sahibayn's view that it is khalaf in the ruling. Note how each side defines the locus of substitution.
Both agree that haqiqah and majaz are attributes of wording, not meaning.
2
Understand the condition of possibility
Determine what each side requires for majaz to be valid. The Sahibayn require that the literal meaning be possible in itself; Abu Hanifa does not require this, only that the expression be grammatically sound.
The key phrase: 'وإلا صار الكلام لغواً' – avoiding meaningless speech.
3
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
Apply the two views to the example of saying to an older slave 'This is my son'. Abu Hanifa uses majaz to free the slave; the Sahibayn reject it as meaningless. Identify the reasoning behind each outcome.
Abu Hanifa: 'ابن' is a necessary concomitant of freedom here.
4
Trace the argument for each side
Examine the dalil for each position. Abu Hanifa: majaz is substitution in wording, so grammatical validity suffices. Sahibayn: substitution must be in ruling, so literal possibility is prerequisite.
The text uses the phrase 'خلف عن الحقيقة في حق اللفظ' vs 'في حق الحكم'.
