Chapter on Haqiqah and Majaz. The product from it, he does not break his oath according to him [Abu Hanifa], while according to them [the two companions] it is diverted to what the wheat contains by way of the generality of majaz, so he breaks his oath by eating it and by eating bread produced from it. Similarly, if he swore 'I will not drink from the Euphrates', according to Abu Hanifa it is diverted to drinking from it by sipping directly, while according to them it is diverted to the customary majaz, which is drinking its water by any means. Then, according to Abu Hanifa, the locus of majaz is substitution for haqiqah in respect of the wording, while according to them: substitution for haqiqah in respect of the ruling, such that even if the haqiqah is possible in itself but acting upon it is prevented by an impediment, one resorts to majaz, otherwise speech would be meaningless. According to him, one resorts to majaz even if the haqiqah is not possible in itself. Example: if he says to his slave who is older than him 'This is my son', according to them one does not resort to majaz due to the impossibility of haqiqah, while according to him one resorts to majaz, so the slave is freed. And upon this the ruling is derived in his statement 'I owe him one thousand' or 'on this wall', and his statement... Then, majaz etc.: Know that there is no disagreement that majaz is substitution for haqiqah, evidenced by the fact that it is only established when the meaning of haqiqah is absent and acting upon it is impossible, and that for the establishment of substitution, the conception of the original is necessary, and that haqiqah and majaz are attributes of the wording, not of the meaning. Hence they said: haqiqah is a word used for such-and-such, and majaz is a word used for such-and-such. Its clarification: According to Abu Hanifa, the utterance of 'this is a lion' for a brave man is substitution for the utterance of 'this is a lion' for the well-known animal, without consideration of the establishment of substitution to the ruling, then the ruling is established based on the validity of the utterance just as haqiqah is established based on the validity of the utterance. According to them, 'this is a lion' for a brave man is substitution in establishing bravery for his statement 'this is a lion' in establishing [the meaning of] lion-ness. This is the intended meaning of the substitution of the ruling of majaz for the ruling of haqiqah according to them, since it is established that both are attributes of the wording, not of the meaning, by agreement. And it is not intended that the bravery of the brave man is substitution for the lion-ness of the animal. This is what the verifiers have chosen in interpreting substitution according to the two views. According to them, the ruling is the intended object, not the expression itself, and considering substitution and originality in what is intended is more appropriate than considering it in other than it. And for Abu Hanifa: haqiqah and majaz are of the genus of words by consensus of the people of language. So majaz is a word that substitutes for a word of haqiqah. And the fruit of the disagreement appears in the condition for establishing majaz: possibility of haqiqah in itself is required according to them, such that if it is not possible, one does not resort to majaz and speech becomes meaningless. According to him, one resorts to majaz even if haqiqah is not possible in itself, as in al-Fusul. 'In respect of the wording' means the consequence of the ruling of majaz requires the validity of speech according to Arabic grammar in terms of containing a subject and predicate, agreement in gender and number, regardless of the possibility of the ruling of haqiqah in it. 'This is my son' etc.: his statement 'this is my son' intended for freedom is substitution for 'this is my son' intended for sonship. So the slave is freed according to him because what makes metaphor valid with this speech exists, which is the soundness of the original from the perspective of Arabic grammar; for this speech is correct in expression as a subject and predicate placed for establishing a ruling, and acting upon haqiqah is impossible because it is impossible for the child to be older than the parent, so majaz is determined, intending freedom by way of mentioning the concomitant and intending the necessary. According to them: majaz is substitution for haqiqah in respect of the ruling, i.e., the ruling of 'this is my son' intended for freedom is substitution for its ruling intended for sonship. So the original should be in its place valid, necessitating the ruling potentially, but acting upon it is prevented by an impediment, so one resorts to majaz. So according to them, this speech is meaningless and the slave is not freed because the possibility of the real meaning, which is a condition for the validity of majaz, does not exist in this speech; for the older cannot be the son of the younger, so it is not taken as majaz which is freedom. So remember. The slave is freed because majaz is substitution in utterance and his statement 'this is my son' is correct speech placed for establishing meaning by form, namely sonship, except that haqiqah is prevented here, so one resorts to majaz which is freedom, because it is the necessary consequence of sonship, so he is freed. And upon this: i.e., based on the aforementioned principle that majaz is substitution for haqiqah in respect of utterance according to him, and in respect of ruling according to them, the following branches are derived. [Al-Shafi: p. 73] 'I owe on this wall': so according to them speech becomes meaningless because the reality of the speech is the obligation of one thousand on one of them without specification, which is impossible in itself because one of them is not a locus; according to him one resorts to majaz and intends by it what it can bear: freedom and obligation of one thousand.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) regarding the nature of majaz (metaphorical usage) and its relationship to haqiqah (literal usage). The core issue is whether majaz requires the possibility of the literal meaning in the situation. The text first presents two examples: swearing not to eat wheat (where eating the grain itself is the literal meaning, but bread is metaphorical) and swearing not to drink from the Euphrates (where drinking by sipping is literal, but drinking by any means is metaphorical). In both, Abu Hanifa restricts to the literal, while the Sahibayn adopt the common metaphorical usage based on customary practice. Then the passage explains the deeper theoretical disagreement: Abu Hanifa holds that majaz substitutes for haqiqah in the wording itself—i.e., the metaphorical expression takes the place of the literal expression, provided the literal expression would be grammatically and semantically valid. The Sahibayn hold that majaz substitutes for haqiqah in the ruling—i.e., the metaphorical meaning takes the place of the literal meaning's ruling, but this requires that the literal meaning be possible in itself. The practical consequence appears in the case where literal meaning is impossible: Abu Hanifa still resorts to majaz to avoid meaningless speech, while the Sahibayn deem such speech meaningless. The famous example is saying to an older slave 'This is my son' intending to free him. Since it is impossible for an older man to be the son of a younger man, the literal meaning is impossible. Abu Hanifa uses majaz to interpret it as freeing the slave (since freedom is a necessary concomitant of sonship in this context). The Sahibayn reject this because the literal possibility is absent, so the speech is void and the slave is not freed. The same logic applies to saying 'I owe one thousand on this wall'—impossible literally, so Abu Hanifa interprets it as an obligation, while the Sahibayn treat it as meaningless. The passage further clarifies that both schools agree haqiqah and majaz are attributes of the wording, not of the meaning; the disagreement is about where the substitution occurs: in the wording itself or in the ruling derived from the wording.

Key Terms
الحقيقة (al-haqiqah): Literal usage of a word in its original, conventional meaning.
المجاز (al-majaz): Metaphorical usage of a word for a meaning other than its literal one, due to a connection and convention.
الخلف (al-khalaf): Substitution: the metaphorical expression or ruling takes the place of the literal one.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) No, because the literal meaning is impossible.
C) Yes, if the slave is younger.
D) No, because such statements are void.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi internal dispute on majaz centers on whether substitution occurs in wording (Abu Hanifa) or ruling (Sahibayn), affecting the condition of possibility of the literal meaning.

Definition and Conditions of Isti'ara between Cause and Effect
Definition and condition of isti'ara between cause and effect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of isti'ara between cause and effect and apply the condition that reverse metaphor (effect for cause) requires exclusivity of the effect to the cause.

Core Idea
Metaphor from cause to effect is always valid; from effect to cause only if the effect is exclusive to that cause.

This lesson introduces isti'ara (metaphor) in legal language, specifically the relationship between cause (sabab) and effect (musabbab). Understanding when a metaphor is valid helps you interpret legal declarations correctly. The source passage defines two directions and establishes a key condition for the reverse direction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two directions
Recognize that isti'ara operates in two directions: from cause (sabab) to effect (musabbab), and from effect to cause. The text gives examples: 'you are a regret' (effect for cause) meaning divorce, and 'I sold myself to you' (cause for effect) meaning marriage.
The default is that cause to effect is always valid.
2
Understand the rule for reverse metaphor
From cause to effect is always permissible. From effect to cause is only permissible when the effect is specific (exclusive) to that cause, i.e., it cannot arise from any other cause.
This is a key limiting condition.
3
Analyze the Quranic example
In Quran 12:36, 'I see myself pressing wine' (أَرَانِي أَعْصِرُ خَمْراً), the word 'khamr' (wine) is used metaphorically for grapes because wine is exclusively derived from grapes.
This verse illustrates the reverse metaphor condition.
4
Distinguish valid from invalid uses
Valid examples: 'you are a regret' for divorce (effect for cause, regret is associated exclusively? Actually the text does not specify exclusivity; rather it's given as an example of cause to effect? Wait: in the source, 'you are a regret' is given as an example of cause for effect? Let me check the translation: 'saying: You are a regret meaning you are divorced' – this is using an effect (regret) for cause (divorce)? Actually regret is a consequence of divorce, so effect for cause. The text says this is permissible. However, the condition of exclusivity might be implied by custom. The key is that for reverse metaphor, exclusivity must be clear.
Invalid: 'you are divorced' meaning 'you are free' – effect (divorce) is not exclusive to cause (freedom) because divorce can occur without freedom.
5
Apply the condition to new examples
Given a statement like 'I have freed you' intended as divorce (cause to effect), it is valid. But 'I have divorced you' intended as freeing is not valid unless the effect is exclusive.
This will be explored in the next lesson.

فصل في تعريف طريق الاستعارة
مثال الأول: فيما إذا قال: "إن ملكتُ عبداً فهو حرٌّ"، فملك نصف العبد فباعه، ثم ملك النصف الآخر لم يُعتق؛ إذ لم يجتمع في ملكه كل العبد. ولو قال: إن اشتريتُ عبداً فهو حر فاشترى نصف العبد فباعه ثم اشترى النصف الآخر عتق النصف الثاني. ولو عنى بالملك الشراء أو بالشراء الملك صحت نيته بطريق المجاز؛ لأن الشراء علة الملك، والملك حكمه، فعمَّت الاستعارة بين العلة والمعلول من الطرفين، إلا أنه فيما يكون تخفيفاً في الشراء. وهو استعارةُ الأصل إلخ أي السبب للحكم دون عكسه، بأن يقول: أنت حسرة يريد به أنت طالق، أو تقول: بعت نفسي منك وتريد به النكاح ولا يجوز أن يقول: أنت طالق ويريد به أنت حرة، وأن يقول: نكحتك ويريد بعتك؛ لأن المسبب محتاج إلى السبب من حيث الثبوت، والمسبب لا يحتاج إليه من حيث الشرعية؛ لأن العتاق لم يشرع إلا لأجل زوال ملك الرقبة وزوال ملك المتعة إنما حصل معه اتفاقاً في بعض الأحيان، وكذا البيع إنما شرع لملك الرقبة، وحل الوطء إنما حصل معه اتفاقا في بعض الأحوال أي فيما إذا كان المبيع أمة، فلا يجوز أن يذكر المسبب ويراد به السبب، إلا إذا كان المسبب مختصاً بالسبب كقوله تعالى: أَرَانِي أَعْصِرُ خَمْراً الآية، فإن الخمر لا يكون إلا من العنب، فينبيء الافتقار من الجانبين كذا في "نور الأنوار". إذ لم يجتمع إلخ: لأنه بعد ملك النصف الآخر لا يوصف بملك العبد، ولا يقال: إنه مالك العبد عرفاً ألا ترى أن رجلاً يقول: والله ما ملكت مائتي درهم قط، ولعله ملكها وزيادة متفرقة، لكن لما لم يجتمع في ملكه مائنا درهم يكون صادقاً كذا في "المعدن". عتق النصف الثاني إلخ ومدار الفرق بين هاتين المسألتين على أصل متقرر في الشرع، وهو أن المطلق قد يتقيد بدلالة العرف والعادة أيضاً كمطلق اسم الدرهم يتقيد بنقد البلد، فمطلق الملك يتقيد بالإجماع بحكم العرف أيضاً، وفي الشراء لم يوجد مثل هذا العرف، فيبقى على إطلاقه فافهم. صحت نيته: يعني إن عنى بالملك الشراء لا يشترط اجتماع النصفين في ملكه فيعتق النصف ويصدق ديانة وقضاء؛ لأنه استعارة الحكم للعلة فيجوز، وفيه تغليظ عليه فيصدقه القاضي. وإن عنى بالشراء الملك يشترط الاجتماع فيه أي في ملكه، فلا يعتق النصف الباقي، ويصدق ديانة؛ لأنه استعارة العلة لحكمها فيحوز، ولكن لا يصدقه القاضي؛ لأنه نوى ما فيه تخفيف عليه فلا يقبل قوله؛ للتهمة لا لعدم صحة الاستعارة كما ذكره المصنف له.

فصل في تعريف طريق الاستعارة حقه لا يُصَدَّقُ في حق القضاء خاصةً لمعنى التهمة، لا لعدم صحة الاستعارة. ومثال الثاني: إذا قال لامرأته: "حرَّرتُك" ونوى به الطلاق يصح؛ لأن التحرير بحقيقته الاستعارة بين السبب والحكم يُوجب زوال ملك البضع بواسطة زوال ملك الرقبة، فكان سبباً محضاً لزوال ملك المتعة، فجاز أن يُستعار عن الطلاق الذي هو مزيل لملك المتعة، ولا يُقال: لو جُعل مجازاً عن لفظ "حررتك" التحرير لوجب أن يكون الطلاق الواقع به رجعيًّا كصريح الطلاق؛ لأنا نقول: لا نَجْعلُه مجازاً عن الطلاق، بل عن المزيل لملك المتعة، وذلك في البائن؛ إذ الرجعي لا يُزيل ملك المتعة عندنا. لا يُصَدَّقُ إلخ: في دعوى الاستعارة؛ لما فيه تخفيف عليه، ولأنه ادعى خلاف الظاهر، وهو وإن كان أميناً مقبول فيه؛ لأن البحث فيما يتضرر هو على تقدير إبقاء اللفظ على الحقيقة، وقول المتهم غير مقبول. لأن التحرير بحقيقته إلخ: يعني أن التحرير بحقيقته يوجب زوال ملك الرقبة وبواسطة زوال الرقبة يوجب زوال ملك البضع، فكان التحرير سبباً محضاً لزوال ملك المتعة؛ لكونه مفضياً إليه لا علة له؛ لأن العلة هي التي لا يتخلل بينها وبين الحكم واسطة، ولأن العلة لا تنفك عن الحكم، والتحرير قد يوجد بدون زوال ملك المتعة كتحرير العبد، والأخت من الرضاعة ونحوهما كذا في "المعدن". فجاز أن يُستعار إلخ على طريق ذكر السبب وإرادة المسبب، وإنما يحتاج إلى النية؛ لأن المحل المضاف إليه غير متعين لهذا المجاز، فهو يحتمل حقيقة وصف الخدمة إلى النية لتعيين المجاز، بخلاف استعارة ألفاظ التمليك للنكاح، فإن إضافتها إلى الحرة لا تدل إلا على النكاح وكذا استعارة هذا ابني؛ لأن إضافته إلى العبد لا تدل إلا على الحرية. ولا يُقال إلخ حاصل الإيراد هو توهم أنه أي قوله: حررتك استعارة للطلاق، فيكون اللفظ أي قوله: حررتك" نائباً مناب "طلقتك"، والنائب له حكم المنوب عنه وهو الطلاق الصريح، وبه يقع الرجعي دون البائن فيلزم أن يصح الرجعة مع أنها لا تصح ومحصول الجواب: أن المسبب ليس هو الطلاق الصريح حتى يقع به الرجعي، بل زوال ملك المتعة، فيكون استعارة عن معنى المزيل لملك المتعة، ولا يخفى أنه مصداقه إنما هو الطلاق البائن، فيؤول الأمر إلى أنه أي حررتك" مستعار للبائن؛ لأنه هو المزيل لملك المتعة لا الرجعي؛ لأنه لا يزيل ملك المتعة للزوج، وإلا لم يصح الرجعة له، فمنشاء الاشتباه فاسد فكذا هو مبني عليه كذا في "الفصول".
Chapter on defining the way of isti'ara (metaphor). First example: When someone says: 'If I own a slave, he is free,' then he owns half the slave and sells it, then owns the other half, the slave is not freed because the entire slave was not in his ownership at once. And if he said: 'If I buy a slave, he is free,' and he buys half and sells it, then buys the other half, the second half is freed. And if he meant by 'ownership' purchase or by 'purchase' ownership, his intention is valid by way of metaphor; because purchase is the cause of ownership, and ownership is its effect, so metaphor is common between cause and effect from both sides, except that it is a lightening in purchase. It is the metaphor of the origin i.e. the cause for the effect, not its opposite, such as saying: 'You are a regret' meaning 'you are divorced,' or saying: 'I sold myself to you' meaning marriage; and it is not permissible to say: 'You are divorced' meaning 'you are free,' or say: 'I married you' meaning 'I sold you'; because the effect needs the cause in terms of existence, but the cause does not need the effect in terms of legislation; because emancipation was legislated only for removing ownership of the neck, and removal of ownership of mut'ah only happened incidentally sometimes, similarly sale was legislated for ownership of the neck, and permissibility of intercourse happened incidentally in some cases i.e. when the sold item is a slave girl. So it is not permissible to mention the effect and intend the cause, unless the effect is specific to the cause like the Almighty's saying: 'I see myself pressing wine' (Quran 12:36) because wine only comes from grapes, so it indicates need from both sides as in 'Nur al-Anwar.' Because after owning the other half, he is not described as owning the slave, and it is not said that he is the owner of the slave customarily. Do you not see that a man says: 'By Allah, I never owned two hundred dirhams,' yet perhaps he owned them and more scattered, but because two hundred dirhams were not in his ownership at once, he is truthful? As in 'al-Ma'din.' The second half is freed etc. And the basis of the difference between these two issues is a settled principle in Shariah: that the absolute may be restricted by the indication of custom and habit as well, just as the absolute term 'dirham' is restricted by the coinage of the country. So the absolute 'ownership' is restricted by consensus due to custom as well, but in purchase there is no such custom, so it remains unrestricted. Understand. His intention is valid: meaning if he intended by 'ownership' purchase, then joining of the two halves in his ownership is not required, so the half is freed, and he is believed both religiously and judicially because it is metaphor of effect for cause, so it is permissible, and it is a severity on him, so the judge believes him. And if he intended by 'purchase' ownership, then joining is required i.e. in his ownership, so the remaining half is not freed, and he is believed religiously because it is metaphor of cause for effect, so it is permissible, but the judge does not believe him because he intended what is lighter on him, so his statement is not accepted due to suspicion, not because the metaphor is invalid, as the author mentioned.

Chapter on defining the way of metaphor. He is not believed specifically in judicial matters due to suspicion, not because the metaphor is invalid. Second example: When a man says to his wife: 'I have freed you' and intends divorce, it is valid; because 'freeing' in its literal sense as metaphor between cause and effect necessitates removal of ownership of sexual right via removal of ownership of the neck, so it is a pure cause for removal of ownership of mut'ah, so it is permissible to use it metaphorically for divorce which is the remover of ownership of mut'ah. And it is not said: If it were made a metaphor for the word 'divorce,' then the divorce occurring by it would have to be revocable like explicit divorce; because we say: We do not make it a metaphor for divorce, but for the remover of ownership of mut'ah, and that is in irrevocable divorce; since revocable divorce does not remove ownership of mut'ah in our school. He is not believed etc. in claiming metaphor because it is lighter on him, and because he claimed contrary to apparent meaning, and even if he is trustworthy, his word is accepted because the discussion is about harm on the assumption of keeping the wording on its literal meaning, and the word of an accused person is not accepted. Because 'freeing' in its literal meaning means: that 'freeing' in its literal sense necessitates removal of ownership of the neck and via removal of the neck necessitates removal of ownership of sexual right, so 'freeing' is a pure cause for removal of ownership of mut'ah because it leads to it, not a 'illa (cause) because the 'illa is that which has no intermediary between it and the ruling, and because the 'illa does not separate from the ruling, and freeing may exist without removal of ownership of mut'ah like freeing a slave, or a foster sister and similar, as in 'al-Ma'din.' So it is permissible to use it metaphorically etc. in the way of mentioning the cause and intending the effect, but it needs intention because the locus to which it is added is not specified for this metaphor, so it is possible for the literal meaning describing service; so intention is needed to specify the metaphor, unlike the metaphor of words of ownership for marriage, because their addition to a free woman indicates nothing but marriage, and similarly the metaphor 'this is my son' because its addition to a slave indicates nothing but freedom. And it is not said etc. The sum of the objection is the misconception that his saying 'I have freed you' is a metaphor for divorce, so the wording 'I have freed you' stands in place of 'I have divorced you,' and the substitute has the ruling of the substituted which is explicit divorce, and by it revocable divorce occurs, not irrevocable, so it would necessitate that reconciliation is valid even though it is not. And the gist of the answer: The effect is not explicit divorce so that revocable divorce occurs by it, rather it is removal of ownership of mut'ah, so it is a metaphor for the meaning of remover of ownership of mut'ah, and it is clear that its instance is irrevocable divorce; because it is the remover of ownership of mut'ah, not revocable because it does not remove ownership of mut'ah for the husband, otherwise reconciliation would not be valid for him. So the source of confusion is corrupt, and thus what is built upon it is also corrupt, as in 'al-Fusul.'
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage is a definition of isti'ara (metaphor) in legal usage, specifically the metaphor between sabab (cause) and musabbab (effect). The author presents two examples from Hanafi fiqh. The first example concerns a conditional emancipation. If a man says 'If I own a slave, he is free,' and then he owns half, sells it, then owns the other half, the slave is not freed because the entire slave was never in his ownership at once. The absolute term 'ownership' here is restricted by custom: one is not called 'owner' of a slave unless he owns the whole slave. But if he says 'If I buy a slave, he is free,' and he buys half, sells it, then buys the other half, the second half is freed because 'purchase' is not customarily restricted to purchasing the whole. The difference hinges on custom (urf). The author then explains that if the speaker intends metaphorically by 'ownership' purchase or vice versa, his intention is valid because metaphor between cause and effect is permissible, but the judge may not accept it if it benefits the speaker (due to suspicion). The second example is a man saying 'I have freed you' to his wife, intending divorce. This is valid because 'freeing' (tahrir) is a cause for removing ownership of the sexual right (milk al-mut'ah), and divorce is the effect that removes that right. However, this can only work for irrevocable divorce (talaq bain) because revocable divorce does not remove ownership of mut'ah entirely. The metaphor is from cause to effect, not from effect to cause, which would be invalid unless the effect is specific to the cause (like wine from grapes). The commentary from 'Nur al-Anwar', 'al-Ma'din', and 'al-Fusul' reinforces these points.

Key Terms
الاستعارة (al-isti'ara): Metaphor: using a word for something other than its original meaning due to a relationship (here between cause and effect).
السبب (al-sabab): The cause that legally brings about an effect (e.g., purchase causes ownership; tahrir causes removal of ownership).
المسبب (al-musabbab): The legal effect produced by the cause (e.g., ownership from purchase; divorce from intention of tahrir).
الملك (al-milk): Ownership; in this context, full ownership of a slave (entire slave) is required by custom.
الطلاق الرجعي (al-talaq al-raj'i): Revocable divorce where the husband retains ownership of mut'a during iddah; it does not remove milk al-mut'a.
التهمة (al-tuhma): Suspicion that the speaker is making a self-serving claim; it prevents judicial acceptance of a metaphorical intention.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What condition must be met for a metaphor from effect (musabbab) to cause (sabab) to be valid?
A) The cause must be unknown.
B) The effect must be exclusive to the cause.
C) The speaker must explicitly state his intention.
D) The listener must be a scholar.

Key Takeaway
Isti'ara from effect to cause is only permissible when the effect is exclusive to that cause, as exemplified by the Quranic 'wine' for grapes.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
advanced
10 min
Applying Isti'ara in Conditional Emancipation and Divorce Declarations
Practical application of isti'ara and custom in legal declarations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rules of isti'ara to conditional emancipation and divorce declarations, and determine both the religious validity and judicial acceptance of metaphorical intentions.

Core Idea
Custom restricts absolute terms; judicial acceptance of metaphorical intention depends on whether it benefits or burdens the speaker.

This lesson works through two detailed examples from Hanafi fiqh: a conditional emancipation with 'ownership' vs. 'purchase', and a declaration of 'freeing' intended as divorce. You will see how custom (urf) restricts absolute terms and how suspicion (tuhma) affects judicial belief. These examples illustrate the practical application of isti'ara rules.

Guided Path
1
Examine the first example: 'If I own a slave, he is free'
A man says: 'If I own a slave, he is free.' He then owns half, sells it, then later owns the other half. The slave is not freed because the absolute term 'own' is restricted by custom: one is not called 'owner' of a slave unless he owns the entire slave at once.
Custom (urf) restricts the absolute meaning.
2
Contrast with 'If I buy a slave, he is free'
If the man says 'If I buy a slave, he is free,' then buys half, sells it, then buys the other half, the second half is freed. 'Buy' is not customarily restricted to buying the whole; one can buy a half-share. So the absolute term remains unrestricted.
No customary restriction for 'buy'.
3
Understand the role of custom (urf) in restricting absolutes
The difference hinges on a settled principle: absolute terms may be restricted by custom. Just as 'dirham' is restricted to the local coinage, 'ownership' is restricted by custom to full ownership. Purchase is not so restricted.
This is independent of metaphor; it is a matter of the literal meaning's scope.
4
Apply metaphor if intention differs from literal meaning
If the speaker intended 'purchase' by 'ownership' (effect for cause) or vice versa (cause for effect), his intention can be valid via isti'ara. However, judicial acceptance depends on whether the metaphor benefits or burdens the speaker.
Cause-to-effect metaphor (e.g., 'purchase' for 'ownership') leads to burden (slave is freed), so judge believes him. Effect-to-cause metaphor (e.g., 'ownership' for 'purchase') leads to benefit (slave not freed), so judge does not believe him due to tuhma.
5
Analyze the second example: 'I have freed you' intended as divorce
A man says to his wife: 'I have freed you' (حررتك) intending divorce. Tahrir is a cause that removes ownership of the neck; it indirectly removes sexual ownership (effect). This is a cause-to-effect metaphor, valid. The resulting divorce is bain (irrevocable) because only bain removes milk al-mut'a entirely.
Raj'i divorce does not remove milk al-mut'a, so metaphor cannot yield raj'i.
6
Understand judicial non-acceptance due to tuhma
In the second example, the judge does not accept the man's claim that he intended divorce because it benefits him (avoids explicit divorce). However, religiously the divorce is valid if his intention was genuine. This is due to tuhma (suspicion), not because the metaphor is invalid.
The metaphor is valid linguistically and religiously.

فصل في تعريف طريق الاستعارة
مثال الأول: فيما إذا قال: "إن ملكتُ عبداً فهو حرٌّ"، فملك نصف العبد فباعه، ثم ملك النصف الآخر لم يُعتق؛ إذ لم يجتمع في ملكه كل العبد. ولو قال: إن اشتريتُ عبداً فهو حر فاشترى نصف العبد فباعه ثم اشترى النصف الآخر عتق النصف الثاني. ولو عنى بالملك الشراء أو بالشراء الملك صحت نيته بطريق المجاز؛ لأن الشراء علة الملك، والملك حكمه، فعمَّت الاستعارة بين العلة والمعلول من الطرفين، إلا أنه فيما يكون تخفيفاً في الشراء. وهو استعارةُ الأصل إلخ أي السبب للحكم دون عكسه، بأن يقول: أنت حسرة يريد به أنت طالق، أو تقول: بعت نفسي منك وتريد به النكاح ولا يجوز أن يقول: أنت طالق ويريد به أنت حرة، وأن يقول: نكحتك ويريد بعتك؛ لأن المسبب محتاج إلى السبب من حيث الثبوت، والمسبب لا يحتاج إليه من حيث الشرعية؛ لأن العتاق لم يشرع إلا لأجل زوال ملك الرقبة وزوال ملك المتعة إنما حصل معه اتفاقاً في بعض الأحيان، وكذا البيع إنما شرع لملك الرقبة، وحل الوطء إنما حصل معه اتفاقا في بعض الأحوال أي فيما إذا كان المبيع أمة، فلا يجوز أن يذكر المسبب ويراد به السبب، إلا إذا كان المسبب مختصاً بالسبب كقوله تعالى: أَرَانِي أَعْصِرُ خَمْراً الآية، فإن الخمر لا يكون إلا من العنب، فينبيء الافتقار من الجانبين كذا في "نور الأنوار". إذ لم يجتمع إلخ: لأنه بعد ملك النصف الآخر لا يوصف بملك العبد، ولا يقال: إنه مالك العبد عرفاً ألا ترى أن رجلاً يقول: والله ما ملكت مائتي درهم قط، ولعله ملكها وزيادة متفرقة، لكن لما لم يجتمع في ملكه مائنا درهم يكون صادقاً كذا في "المعدن". عتق النصف الثاني إلخ ومدار الفرق بين هاتين المسألتين على أصل متقرر في الشرع، وهو أن المطلق قد يتقيد بدلالة العرف والعادة أيضاً كمطلق اسم الدرهم يتقيد بنقد البلد، فمطلق الملك يتقيد بالإجماع بحكم العرف أيضاً، وفي الشراء لم يوجد مثل هذا العرف، فيبقى على إطلاقه فافهم. صحت نيته: يعني إن عنى بالملك الشراء لا يشترط اجتماع النصفين في ملكه فيعتق النصف ويصدق ديانة وقضاء؛ لأنه استعارة الحكم للعلة فيجوز، وفيه تغليظ عليه فيصدقه القاضي. وإن عنى بالشراء الملك يشترط الاجتماع فيه أي في ملكه، فلا يعتق النصف الباقي، ويصدق ديانة؛ لأنه استعارة العلة لحكمها فيحوز، ولكن لا يصدقه القاضي؛ لأنه نوى ما فيه تخفيف عليه فلا يقبل قوله؛ للتهمة لا لعدم صحة الاستعارة كما ذكره المصنف له.

فصل في تعريف طريق الاستعارة حقه لا يُصَدَّقُ في حق القضاء خاصةً لمعنى التهمة، لا لعدم صحة الاستعارة. ومثال الثاني: إذا قال لامرأته: "حرَّرتُك" ونوى به الطلاق يصح؛ لأن التحرير بحقيقته الاستعارة بين السبب والحكم يُوجب زوال ملك البضع بواسطة زوال ملك الرقبة، فكان سبباً محضاً لزوال ملك المتعة، فجاز أن يُستعار عن الطلاق الذي هو مزيل لملك المتعة، ولا يُقال: لو جُعل مجازاً عن لفظ "حررتك" التحرير لوجب أن يكون الطلاق الواقع به رجعيًّا كصريح الطلاق؛ لأنا نقول: لا نَجْعلُه مجازاً عن الطلاق، بل عن المزيل لملك المتعة، وذلك في البائن؛ إذ الرجعي لا يُزيل ملك المتعة عندنا. لا يُصَدَّقُ إلخ: في دعوى الاستعارة؛ لما فيه تخفيف عليه، ولأنه ادعى خلاف الظاهر، وهو وإن كان أميناً مقبول فيه؛ لأن البحث فيما يتضرر هو على تقدير إبقاء اللفظ على الحقيقة، وقول المتهم غير مقبول. لأن التحرير بحقيقته إلخ: يعني أن التحرير بحقيقته يوجب زوال ملك الرقبة وبواسطة زوال الرقبة يوجب زوال ملك البضع، فكان التحرير سبباً محضاً لزوال ملك المتعة؛ لكونه مفضياً إليه لا علة له؛ لأن العلة هي التي لا يتخلل بينها وبين الحكم واسطة، ولأن العلة لا تنفك عن الحكم، والتحرير قد يوجد بدون زوال ملك المتعة كتحرير العبد، والأخت من الرضاعة ونحوهما كذا في "المعدن". فجاز أن يُستعار إلخ على طريق ذكر السبب وإرادة المسبب، وإنما يحتاج إلى النية؛ لأن المحل المضاف إليه غير متعين لهذا المجاز، فهو يحتمل حقيقة وصف الخدمة إلى النية لتعيين المجاز، بخلاف استعارة ألفاظ التمليك للنكاح، فإن إضافتها إلى الحرة لا تدل إلا على النكاح وكذا استعارة هذا ابني؛ لأن إضافته إلى العبد لا تدل إلا على الحرية. ولا يُقال إلخ حاصل الإيراد هو توهم أنه أي قوله: حررتك استعارة للطلاق، فيكون اللفظ أي قوله: حررتك" نائباً مناب "طلقتك"، والنائب له حكم المنوب عنه وهو الطلاق الصريح، وبه يقع الرجعي دون البائن فيلزم أن يصح الرجعة مع أنها لا تصح ومحصول الجواب: أن المسبب ليس هو الطلاق الصريح حتى يقع به الرجعي، بل زوال ملك المتعة، فيكون استعارة عن معنى المزيل لملك المتعة، ولا يخفى أنه مصداقه إنما هو الطلاق البائن، فيؤول الأمر إلى أنه أي حررتك" مستعار للبائن؛ لأنه هو المزيل لملك المتعة لا الرجعي؛ لأنه لا يزيل ملك المتعة للزوج، وإلا لم يصح الرجعة له، فمنشاء الاشتباه فاسد فكذا هو مبني عليه كذا في "الفصول".
Chapter on defining the way of isti'ara (metaphor). First example: When someone says: 'If I own a slave, he is free,' then he owns half the slave and sells it, then owns the other half, the slave is not freed because the entire slave was not in his ownership at once. And if he said: 'If I buy a slave, he is free,' and he buys half and sells it, then buys the other half, the second half is freed. And if he meant by 'ownership' purchase or by 'purchase' ownership, his intention is valid by way of metaphor; because purchase is the cause of ownership, and ownership is its effect, so metaphor is common between cause and effect from both sides, except that it is a lightening in purchase. It is the metaphor of the origin i.e. the cause for the effect, not its opposite, such as saying: 'You are a regret' meaning 'you are divorced,' or saying: 'I sold myself to you' meaning marriage; and it is not permissible to say: 'You are divorced' meaning 'you are free,' or say: 'I married you' meaning 'I sold you'; because the effect needs the cause in terms of existence, but the cause does not need the effect in terms of legislation; because emancipation was legislated only for removing ownership of the neck, and removal of ownership of mut'ah only happened incidentally sometimes, similarly sale was legislated for ownership of the neck, and permissibility of intercourse happened incidentally in some cases i.e. when the sold item is a slave girl. So it is not permissible to mention the effect and intend the cause, unless the effect is specific to the cause like the Almighty's saying: 'I see myself pressing wine' (Quran 12:36) because wine only comes from grapes, so it indicates need from both sides as in 'Nur al-Anwar.' Because after owning the other half, he is not described as owning the slave, and it is not said that he is the owner of the slave customarily. Do you not see that a man says: 'By Allah, I never owned two hundred dirhams,' yet perhaps he owned them and more scattered, but because two hundred dirhams were not in his ownership at once, he is truthful? As in 'al-Ma'din.' The second half is freed etc. And the basis of the difference between these two issues is a settled principle in Shariah: that the absolute may be restricted by the indication of custom and habit as well, just as the absolute term 'dirham' is restricted by the coinage of the country. So the absolute 'ownership' is restricted by consensus due to custom as well, but in purchase there is no such custom, so it remains unrestricted. Understand. His intention is valid: meaning if he intended by 'ownership' purchase, then joining of the two halves in his ownership is not required, so the half is freed, and he is believed both religiously and judicially because it is metaphor of effect for cause, so it is permissible, and it is a severity on him, so the judge believes him. And if he intended by 'purchase' ownership, then joining is required i.e. in his ownership, so the remaining half is not freed, and he is believed religiously because it is metaphor of cause for effect, so it is permissible, but the judge does not believe him because he intended what is lighter on him, so his statement is not accepted due to suspicion, not because the metaphor is invalid, as the author mentioned.

Chapter on defining the way of metaphor. He is not believed specifically in judicial matters due to suspicion, not because the metaphor is invalid. Second example: When a man says to his wife: 'I have freed you' and intends divorce, it is valid; because 'freeing' in its literal sense as metaphor between cause and effect necessitates removal of ownership of sexual right via removal of ownership of the neck, so it is a pure cause for removal of ownership of mut'ah, so it is permissible to use it metaphorically for divorce which is the remover of ownership of mut'ah. And it is not said: If it were made a metaphor for the word 'divorce,' then the divorce occurring by it would have to be revocable like explicit divorce; because we say: We do not make it a metaphor for divorce, but for the remover of ownership of mut'ah, and that is in irrevocable divorce; since revocable divorce does not remove ownership of mut'ah in our school. He is not believed etc. in claiming metaphor because it is lighter on him, and because he claimed contrary to apparent meaning, and even if he is trustworthy, his word is accepted because the discussion is about harm on the assumption of keeping the wording on its literal meaning, and the word of an accused person is not accepted. Because 'freeing' in its literal meaning means: that 'freeing' in its literal sense necessitates removal of ownership of the neck and via removal of the neck necessitates removal of ownership of sexual right, so 'freeing' is a pure cause for removal of ownership of mut'ah because it leads to it, not a 'illa (cause) because the 'illa is that which has no intermediary between it and the ruling, and because the 'illa does not separate from the ruling, and freeing may exist without removal of ownership of mut'ah like freeing a slave, or a foster sister and similar, as in 'al-Ma'din.' So it is permissible to use it metaphorically etc. in the way of mentioning the cause and intending the effect, but it needs intention because the locus to which it is added is not specified for this metaphor, so it is possible for the literal meaning describing service; so intention is needed to specify the metaphor, unlike the metaphor of words of ownership for marriage, because their addition to a free woman indicates nothing but marriage, and similarly the metaphor 'this is my son' because its addition to a slave indicates nothing but freedom. And it is not said etc. The sum of the objection is the misconception that his saying 'I have freed you' is a metaphor for divorce, so the wording 'I have freed you' stands in place of 'I have divorced you,' and the substitute has the ruling of the substituted which is explicit divorce, and by it revocable divorce occurs, not irrevocable, so it would necessitate that reconciliation is valid even though it is not. And the gist of the answer: The effect is not explicit divorce so that revocable divorce occurs by it, rather it is removal of ownership of mut'ah, so it is a metaphor for the meaning of remover of ownership of mut'ah, and it is clear that its instance is irrevocable divorce; because it is the remover of ownership of mut'ah, not revocable because it does not remove ownership of mut'ah for the husband, otherwise reconciliation would not be valid for him. So the source of confusion is corrupt, and thus what is built upon it is also corrupt, as in 'al-Fusul.'
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage is a definition of isti'ara (metaphor) in legal usage, specifically the metaphor between sabab (cause) and musabbab (effect). The author presents two examples from Hanafi fiqh. The first example concerns a conditional emancipation. If a man says 'If I own a slave, he is free,' and then he owns half, sells it, then owns the other half, the slave is not freed because the entire slave was never in his ownership at once. The absolute term 'ownership' here is restricted by custom: one is not called 'owner' of a slave unless he owns the whole slave. But if he says 'If I buy a slave, he is free,' and he buys half, sells it, then buys the other half, the second half is freed because 'purchase' is not customarily restricted to purchasing the whole. The difference hinges on custom (urf). The author then explains that if the speaker intends metaphorically by 'ownership' purchase or vice versa, his intention is valid because metaphor between cause and effect is permissible, but the judge may not accept it if it benefits the speaker (due to suspicion). The second example is a man saying 'I have freed you' to his wife, intending divorce. This is valid because 'freeing' (tahrir) is a cause for removing ownership of the sexual right (milk al-mut'ah), and divorce is the effect that removes that right. However, this can only work for irrevocable divorce (talaq bain) because revocable divorce does not remove ownership of mut'ah entirely. The metaphor is from cause to effect, not from effect to cause, which would be invalid unless the effect is specific to the cause (like wine from grapes). The commentary from 'Nur al-Anwar', 'al-Ma'din', and 'al-Fusul' reinforces these points.

Key Terms
الاستعارة (al-isti'ara): Metaphor: using a word for something other than its original meaning due to a relationship (here between cause and effect).
السبب (al-sabab): The cause that legally brings about an effect (e.g., purchase causes ownership; tahrir causes removal of ownership).
المسبب (al-musabbab): The legal effect produced by the cause (e.g., ownership from purchase; divorce from intention of tahrir).
الملك (al-milk): Ownership; in the context of slave ownership, custom restricts it to full ownership.
العرف (al-urf): Custom; in Hanafi usul, custom can qualify absolute language.
التهمة (al-tuhma): Suspicion that the speaker is making a self-serving claim; prevents judicial acceptance of metaphorical intention.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the first example, when the speaker says 'If I own a slave, he is free' and later owns half, sells, then owns the other half, what is the ruling and why?
A) The slave is freed because he eventually owned the whole.
B) The slave is not freed because 'own' is restricted by custom to full ownership at once.
C) The slave is freed only if the speaker intended 'purchase' by 'ownership'.
D) The slave is not freed unless he owned the whole at the moment of the condition.

Key Takeaway
Custom can restrict absolute legal terms, and judicial acceptance of metaphorical intention depends on whether it benefits or burdens the speaker, due to the principle of tuhma.

Sarih and Kinaya: Definitions and Rulings
Defines sarih and kinaya with rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define sarih and kinaya and state the Hanafi ruling that sarih expressions are independent of intention.

Core Idea
Sarih needs no intention; kinaya hides its meaning until context reveals it.

Usul al-Fiqh classifies expressions into explicit (sarih) and allusive (kinaya). This lesson unpacks their definitions and legal effects, showing how a sarih phrase like 'You are divorced' triggers its ruling regardless of the speaker's intent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definitions
Read the Arabic definitions: sarih is 'a word whose intended meaning is apparent', kinaya is 'that whose meaning is hidden'.
Note the contrast: apparent vs. hidden.
2
State the ruling for sarih
The text says sarih 'is independent of intention' and 'necessitates the establishment of its meaning' in any mode (report, description, call).
3
Review examples
Divorce and manumission phrases are sarih: 'You are divorced', 'You are free', etc. They take effect whether the speaker intended or not.
This is the Hanafi position.
4
Understand kinaya's status
Kinaya requires context; metaphor before becoming conventional is treated as kinaya.
Kinaya often needs intention to take effect, unlike sarih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الصريح والكناية: الصريح لفظ يكون المراد به ظاهراً، كقوله بعتُ واشتريت وأمثاله. وحكمه: أنه بذلك اللفظ يُوجب ثبوت معناه بأي طريق كان من إخبار أو نعت أو نداء، ومن حكمه: أنه يستغني عن النية. وعلى هذا قلنا: إذا قال لامرأته: "أنت طالق" أو "طلقتُك" أو "يا طالق" يقع الطلاق، نوى أو لم ينو . وكذا لو قال لعبده: "أنت حر" أو "حرَّرتُك" أو "يا حر". وعلى هذا قلنا: إن التيمم يفيد الطهارة؛ لأن قوله تعالى: ﴿وَلَكِنْ يُرِيدُ لِيُطَهِّرَكُمْ﴾ صريح في حصول الطهارة به، وللشافعي فيه قولان: أحدهما: إنه طهارة ضرورية والآخر: إنه ليس بطهارة بل هو ساتر للحدث. وعلى هذا تُخَرَّج المسائل على المذهبين من جوازه قبل الوقت وأداء الفرضين بتيمم واحد، وإمامة المتيمم للمتوضئين، وجوازه بدون خوف تلف النفس أو العضو بالوضوء، وجوازه للعيد والجنازة، وجوازه بنية الطهارة. والكناية هي ما استتر معناه والمجاز: قبل أن يصير متعارفاً بمنزلة الكناية. ولقوله : "الصعيد الطيب وضوء المسلم ولو إلى عشرين سنين ما لم يجد الماء" الحديث رواه ابن حبان في صحيحه وأصحاب السنن وصححه الترمذي والحاكم حمل عن أبي ذر وفي رواية لأبي داود والترمذي: "طهور المسلم" كذا في الفصول.
Chapter on Explicit and Allusive Expressions: Explicit expression is a word whose intended meaning is apparent, such as one says 'I sold' and 'I bought' and their likes. Its ruling: that by that word it necessitates the establishment of its meaning in any manner—whether report, description, or calling—and among its rulings is that it is independent of intention. And upon this we said: if he says to his wife 'You are divorced' or 'I divorced you' or 'O divorced one', divorce occurs, whether he intended or not. Likewise if he says to his slave 'You are free' or 'I freed you' or 'O free one'. And upon this we said: indeed tayammum benefits purification; because Allah's statement (But He intends to purify you) is explicit in the attainment of purification through it. For al-Shafi'i there are two opinions: one that it is a necessary purification, and the other that it is not purification but rather a coverer of the event. And upon this are derived issues according to the two schools: its permissibility before time, performing two obligatory prayers with one tayammum, the imamate of the mutayammim over those with wudu, its permissibility without fear of loss of life or limb due to wudu, its permissibility for Eid and funeral prayer, and its permissibility with the intention of purification. And allusive expression is that whose meaning is hidden; and metaphor, before it becomes conventional, is of the status of allusion. And there is his statement: 'Pure earth is the wudu of a Muslim even for twenty years as long as he does not find water'—the hadith narrated by Ibn Hibban in his Sahih, and the authors of Sunan, and authenticated by al-Tirmidhi and al-Hakim transmitted from Abu Dharr, and in a narration by Abu Dawud and al-Tirmidhi: 'purification of the Muslim'—as mentioned in al-Fusul.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage defines two key linguistic categories in Hanafi usul: sarih (explicit) and kinaya (allusive). A sarih expression clearly conveys its meaning without needing intention, as in the examples of divorce and manumission. The author applies this to tayammum, arguing that the Quranic verse (5:6) explicitly states that tayammum purifies, making it a full purification (tahara) according to the Hanafis, while Shafi'i has two opinions—that it is only a necessary purification or merely a coverer of the impurity. The passage then lists practical consequences of this difference, such as performing multiple prayers with one tayammum. Kinaya is defined as a word whose meaning is hidden and requires a clue to be understood. The author notes that metaphor, before becoming conventional, functions like kinaya. A supporting hadith is cited to reinforce the Hanafi position on tayammum's purity.

Key Terms
الصريح (al-sarih): An expression whose intended meaning is apparent and independent of intention.
الكناية (al-kinaya): An expression whose meaning is hidden and is known only through a contextual clue.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling of a sarih expression regarding intention?
A) It requires intention for legal effect.
B) It is independent of intention.
C) It requires intention only in divorce cases.
D) It depends on the context.

Key Takeaway
Sarih expressions function automatically; kinaya requires contextual clues or intention to take legal effect.

Applying Sarih to Tayammum: The Hanafi–Shafi'i Dispute
Applies sarih principle to tayammum and discusses inter-madhhab dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the Hanafi school uses the sarih principle to prove tayammum is a full purification, and identify the practical consequences of the dispute with al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
A sarih Quranic verse establishes tayammum as true purification, overriding analogical doubts.

The nature of tayammum—whether it purifies or merely covers impurity—is a key inter-madhhab dispute. Here, the Hanafi school invokes the usul category of sarih: the Quranic expression is so explicit that no further interpretation is needed. This lesson unpacks the dalil, the opposing views, and the practical rulings that hinge on this distinction.

Guided Path
1
Locate the Hanafi claim
The text states: 'And upon this we said: indeed tayammum benefits purification.' The claim is that tayammum is a full purification (tahara).
2
Identify the dalil (proof)
The proof is Quran 5:6: 'But He intends to purify you'. The word 'yutahhirakum' is sarih (explicit) in indicating purification.
The Hanafis argue that explicit wording must be taken literally.
3
Understand the opposing view
Al-Shafi'i has two opinions: one that it is a 'necessary purification', another that it is not purification at all but a 'coverer' (satir) of the impurity.
The text lists the two positions.
4
Examine the practical consequences
The dispute affects several rulings: performing tayammum before the prayer time, doing multiple prayers with one tayammum, and the imamate of a mutayammim over those with wudu.
These are listed in the text as branches derived from each school's position.
5
Consider the supporting hadith
The Prophet's saying 'Pure earth is the wudu of a Muslim' is cited to reinforce the Hanafi view.
The hadith uses 'wudu' which implies purification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الصريح والكناية: الصريح لفظ يكون المراد به ظاهراً، كقوله بعتُ واشتريت وأمثاله. وحكمه: أنه بذلك اللفظ يُوجب ثبوت معناه بأي طريق كان من إخبار أو نعت أو نداء، ومن حكمه: أنه يستغني عن النية. وعلى هذا قلنا: إذا قال لامرأته: "أنت طالق" أو "طلقتُك" أو "يا طالق" يقع الطلاق، نوى أو لم ينو . وكذا لو قال لعبده: "أنت حر" أو "حرَّرتُك" أو "يا حر". وعلى هذا قلنا: إن التيمم يفيد الطهارة؛ لأن قوله تعالى: ﴿وَلَكِنْ يُرِيدُ لِيُطَهِّرَكُمْ﴾ صريح في حصول الطهارة به، وللشافعي فيه قولان: أحدهما: إنه طهارة ضرورية والآخر: إنه ليس بطهارة بل هو ساتر للحدث. وعلى هذا تُخَرَّج المسائل على المذهبين من جوازه قبل الوقت وأداء الفرضين بتيمم واحد، وإمامة المتيمم للمتوضئين، وجوازه بدون خوف تلف النفس أو العضو بالوضوء، وجوازه للعيد والجنازة، وجوازه بنية الطهارة. والكناية هي ما استتر معناه والمجاز: قبل أن يصير متعارفاً بمنزلة الكناية. ولقوله : "الصعيد الطيب وضوء المسلم ولو إلى عشرين سنين ما لم يجد الماء" الحديث رواه ابن حبان في صحيحه وأصحاب السنن وصححه الترمذي والحاكم حمل عن أبي ذر وفي رواية لأبي داود والترمذي: "طهور المسلم" كذا في الفصول.
Chapter on Explicit and Allusive Expressions: Explicit expression is a word whose intended meaning is apparent, such as one says 'I sold' and 'I bought' and their likes. Its ruling: that by that word it necessitates the establishment of its meaning in any manner—whether report, description, or calling—and among its rulings is that it is independent of intention. And upon this we said: if he says to his wife 'You are divorced' or 'I divorced you' or 'O divorced one', divorce occurs, whether he intended or not. Likewise if he says to his slave 'You are free' or 'I freed you' or 'O free one'. And upon this we said: indeed tayammum benefits purification; because Allah's statement (But He intends to purify you) is explicit in the attainment of purification through it. For al-Shafi'i there are two opinions: one that it is a necessary purification, and the other that it is not purification but rather a coverer of the event. And upon this are derived issues according to the two schools: its permissibility before time, performing two obligatory prayers with one tayammum, the imamate of the mutayammim over those with wudu, its permissibility without fear of loss of life or limb due to wudu, its permissibility for Eid and funeral prayer, and its permissibility with the intention of purification. And allusive expression is that whose meaning is hidden; and metaphor, before it becomes conventional, is of the status of allusion. And there is his statement: 'Pure earth is the wudu of a Muslim even for twenty years as long as he does not find water'—the hadith narrated by Ibn Hibban in his Sahih, and the authors of Sunan, and authenticated by al-Tirmidhi and al-Hakim transmitted from Abu Dharr, and in a narration by Abu Dawud and al-Tirmidhi: 'purification of the Muslim'—as mentioned in al-Fusul.

Commentary
This passage defines two key linguistic categories in Hanafi usul: sarih (explicit) and kinaya (allusive). A sarih expression clearly conveys its meaning without needing intention, as in the examples of divorce and manumission. The author applies this to tayammum, arguing that the Quranic verse (5:6) explicitly states that tayammum purifies, making it a full purification (tahara) according to the Hanafis, while Shafi'i has two opinions—that it is only a necessary purification or merely a coverer of the impurity. The passage then lists practical consequences of this difference, such as performing multiple prayers with one tayammum. Kinaya is defined as a word whose meaning is hidden and requires a clue to be understood. The author notes that metaphor, before becoming conventional, functions like kinaya. A supporting hadith is cited to reinforce the Hanafi position on tayammum's purity.

Key Terms
التيمم (al-tayammum): Dry ablution with earth as a substitute for wudu or ghusl when water is unavailable or harmful.
طهارة ضرورية (tahara daruriyya): Necessary purification — Shafi'i's first opinion that tayammum is purification but only for necessity.
ساتر للحدث (satir li-l-hadath): A coverer of the impurity — Shafi'i's second opinion that tayammum does not remove impurity but only permits prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, which of the following is a practical consequence of the Hanafi view that tayammum is a full purification?
A) Tayammum cannot be performed before the prayer time.
B) Only one obligatory prayer can be performed with one tayammum.
C) Multiple obligatory prayers can be performed with one tayammum.
D) A mutayammim cannot lead those with wudu in prayer.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi use of sarih to establish tayammum as full purification leads to practical leniency in multiple prayers, imamate, and other rulings.

Sarih and Kinayah: Definitions and Legal Effects
Defining sarih and kinayah with rulings on hudud and divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a statement as sarih or kinayah, apply the ruling that kinayah requires intention or context, and evaluate the Hanafi position that kinayah words like 'bain' produce bā'in divorce.

Core Idea
Kinayah requires intention or context to effect a ruling; sarih operates automatically.

This passage from Usul al-Shashi introduces a fundamental distinction in legal speech: explicit (sarih) and implicit (kinayah). Understanding when a speaker's words directly establish a ruling versus when they need additional evidence is crucial for applying fiqh correctly, especially in sensitive areas like divorce and hudud penalties. We will dissect the definition, the reasoning behind it, and a key difference with the Shafi‘i school.

Guided Path
1
Define sarih and kinayah
From the text, identify the defining characteristics of each: sarih is clear and establishes a ruling without intention; kinayah is ambiguous and requires niyyah (intention) or dalalat al-hal (context).
The text uses divorce words as examples.
2
Analyze the legal effect of kinayah
The passage states that kinayah words like 'bain' and 'haram' do not operate as divorce words; they have their own real meanings. They only produce a legal effect (e.g., bā'in divorce) when intention or context is present, and that effect is not revocable (raj‘i).
3
Apply to hudud penalties
The author explains that hudud (fixed penalties) are not established by kinayah because they are repelled by doubts (shubuhat). The ambiguity in kinayah creates a doubt, so no hadd is imposed even if intention is present.
Example: a confession by kinayah does not lead to a hadd punishment.
4
Compare with Shafi‘i position
The text addresses an objection: if kinayah words are implicit, why do they not produce raj‘i divorce as the Shafi‘i school holds? The Hanafi response is that these words are actual (haqiqah) in their literal meanings, not euphemisms for talaq, so they produce their own effect (bā'in).
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf: practical difference in right of return (ruj‘a).
5
Synthesize the istinbat chain
Trace the derivation: the inherent probability (ihtimal) in kinayah words → requires a factor to resolve ambiguity (niyyah or context) → ruling (bā'in divorce, no hadd).
Compare with the absolute effect of sarih words.

Source Text

الرجال، وأن تكون ممنوعة عن المعاصي، أو عن الخيرات، أو عن الوالدين، أو عن الخروج والبروز، فإذا ثبت الاحتمال فيها من وجوه استر مراده من قوله: مثلاً أنت بائن أو حرام، فلذا كناية ههنا أي في باب الطلاق. لا Section on explicit and implicit speech. The ruling of implicit speech: the establishment of the ruling by it when there is intention, or by contextual indication; for it must have evidence that removes hesitation and strengthens some of its possible meanings. For this meaning, the words 'separation' and 'prohibition' are called implicit speech in the chapter of divorce, due to hesitation and concealment of intent, not that it operates as divorce. And from it branches the ruling of implicit speech regarding the lack of authority of return (ruj'a). And due to the presence of hesitation in implicit speech, penalties are not established by it, such that if one confesses regarding fornication or theft, the penalty is not established by implicit speech even when intention exists, to the extent that the listener knows that the speaker intended one of its meanings by saying 'I intended' or 'I meant such', or knows by contextual indication like implicit words of divorce during discussion of divorce; then divorce occurs by contextual indication, even if the speaker says 'I did not intend divorce'. Unlike explicit speech, for the speaker even if he does not intend a meaning with the word 'divorce' for example, its meaning is established and divorce occurs, for his word stands in place of its meaning, so there is no need for intention, rather intention is present because speech is among voluntary actions, all of which occur by will. It is only implicit speech in the chapter of divorce despite being apparent in meaning in itself, because when it is said 'you are separated' or 'you are forbidden', hesitation and concealment occur; for 'separation' regarding her may be from the connection of marriage, or from sin, or from good deeds, or from their likes in honor, beauty, and piety. Likewise, 'prohibition' may be that she is forbidden to the husband, or to other men, and that she is prevented from sins, or from good deeds, or from parents, or from leaving and appearing. So when probability is established in them from various aspects, his intention is concealed from his statement e.g. 'you are separated' or 'forbidden', so it is implicit here i.e. in the chapter of divorce. 'Not that it operates' etc. refers to the answer to a presumed objection: if these words were implicit for divorce, they would operate as divorce in the occurrence of revocable divorce as al-Shafi'i said, and it is the school of Umar and Abdullah ibn Mas'ud (may Allah be pleased with them), but what occurs by them according to you is irrevocable divorce, so the issue is problematic: they are implicit for divorce. The answer: these words are real in separation and prohibition, so they operate by their requirements, and they are called implicit only due to their probability of aspects like other shared words, so their intended meaning is concealed like concealment in implicit speech, not that they are used euphemistically for explicit divorce. Thus in al-Ma'din. Penalties like the hadd of fornication and hadd of slander, because they are repelled by doubts, and in implicit words there is doubt and deficiency in establishing their cause due to the mentioned concealment. Unless the explicit word is mentioned. For this meaning, the hadd is not imposed on the mute person by gesture. And if a man accuses another of fornication and the other says 'you have told the truth', the hadd is not obligatory on him due to the possibility of him confirming him in something else.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage defines the two categories of speech in legal contexts: sarih (explicit) and kinayah (implicit/allusive). The author begins by stating the hukm of kinayah: a ruling is established by it only when coupled with intention (niyyah) or contextual indication (dalalat al-hal). This is because kinayah inherently carries multiple possible meanings, requiring a factor to remove hesitation. The reason words like 'bain' (separated) and 'haram' (forbidden) are considered kinayah in divorce is their ambiguity; they could refer to separation from marriage, sin, good deeds, etc. Thus, they do not directly effect divorce like the explicit word 'talāq'. Instead, they cause an irrevocable (bā'in) divorce when intended. The passage then explains that such ambiguity prevents the establishment of hudūd penalties (like for fornication or theft) because penalties are repelled by doubts, and kinayah introduces doubt. Even if the speaker intends a meaning, the inherent ambiguity remains a shubha. The text also clarifies that an explicit word is needed for hudūd; even a mute person's gesture does not suffice. Furthermore, if someone responds 'you have told the truth' to an accusation of fornication, it does not constitute a clear confession for hadd. The commentary woven into the text addresses a possible objection from the Shāfi‘ī school: if kinayah words are implicit, why do they not result in revocable (raj‘ī) divorce? The Hanafī response is that these words are real in their literal meanings (separation/prohibition) and thus produce their own legal effects; they are called kinayah only due to multiple probabilities, not because they symbolize explicit divorce.

Key Terms
الصريح (al-ṣarīḥ): Explicit speech whose ruling is established without need for intention; e.g. 'anti ṭāliq'
الكناية (al-kināyah): Implicit speech requiring intention or contextual indication to establish the ruling; ambiguous word with multiple probabilities
النية (al-niyyah): Intention of the speaker, necessary for kinayah to produce a legal effect
دلالة الحال (dalālatu al-ḥāl): Contextual indication that reveals the speaker's intent without explicit expression

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, when does a statement like 'You are forbidden' (anti harām) effect divorce?
A) Always, because it is explicit.
B) Only when the speaker intends divorce and there is no contextual indication.
C) When there is intention or contextual indication, and it results in irrevocable divorce.
D) Never, because such words are not related to divorce.

Key Takeaway
Sarih speech establishes a ruling without need for intention, while kinayah requires intention or context and, in divorce, produces bā'in divorce; hudud are never established by kinayah due to inherent doubt.

Preference of Nass over Zahir: The Tarjih Principle in Hanafi Usul
Explains the hierarchy of textual evidence with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that a nass (unequivocal text) is preferred over a zahir (apparent but ambiguous text) and identify when a text is classified as nass, zahir, or mu'awwal.

Core Idea
When a nass conflicts with a zahir, the nass prevails because it has greater clarity and decisiveness.

This passage from Usul al-Shashi introduces a crucial rule in Hanafi usul: when two texts appear to conflict, the one that is clearer and more decisive (nass) overrides the one that is merely apparent (zahir). The author illustrates this with two sets of Prophetic hadith: one about drinking urine, where a general prohibition (nass) overrides a specific permission (zahir), and another about agricultural tithe, where a clear command for tithe (nass) overrides an ambiguous statement about vegetables (mu'awwal). This principle has direct implications for how rulings are derived and which evidence takes precedence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conflicting texts
Locate the two hadith that appear to contradict each other: the permission for the Urayna tribe to drink urine vs. the general command to avoid urine. Similarly, the general tithe hadith vs. the hadith on vegetables.
The Urayna hadith is a specific concession; the avoidance hadith is general.
2
Classify each text as nass, zahir, or mu'awwal
Determine which text is unequivocal (nass) and which is open to interpretation (zahir) or has been interpreted away from its apparent meaning (mu'awwal). The avoidance hadith is nass for prohibition; the Urayna hadith is zahir for permissibility. The tithe hadith is nass; the vegetable hadith is mu'awwal because 'sadaqah' can mean zakat or charity.
Mu'awwal is a zahir that has been reinterpreted due to conflicting evidence.
3
Apply the tarjih rule: nass over zahir
State the principle: when a nass and a zahir conflict, the nass is given precedence because it is stronger and clearer. The zahir is either reinterpreted (ta'wil) to reconcile or set aside.
This is a fundamental rule in Hanafi usul for resolving textual conflicts.
4
Consider special circumstances and exceptions
Check if the conflicting text (zahir) can be explained as a specific concession or limited application. For the Urayna hadith, it was a limited dispensation for treatment, not a general ruling.
The Urayna case is not a general permission but a one-time concession.
5
Note the practical outcome (thamarah)
Identify the final ruling: drinking urine is absolutely impermissible (haram) except for the specific Urayna example; tithe is due on all cultivated produce including vegetables, contrary to the opponents' view.
The Hanafi position on tithe is based on prioritizing the nass.

فصل في المتقابلات
فيترجح العملُ بالنص. وكذلك قوله عليها لأهل عُرَيْنَةَ: "اشربُوا من أَبْوَالها وألبانها نص في بيان سبب الشفاء، وظاهر في إجازة شرب البول، وقوله عليه: "استنزهوا من البول فإن عامة عذاب القبر منه نص في وجوب الاحتراز عن البول، فيترجَّحُ النص على الظاهر، فلا يحلُّ شربُ البول أصلاً، وقوله عليه: "ما سقته السماء ففيه العُشْرُ" نص في بيان العُشْر. فيترجح العمل بالنص: لأنه أقوى من الظاهر؛ لأن النص فيه ازدياد وضوح على الظاهر كما مر . [الشافي: ص ٩٠] من أَبْوَالها : أي أبوال إبل الصدقات وقلت ما روي أن قوماً من عرينة أتوا المدينة فلم يوافقهم المدينة، ومرضوا وأصفرت ألوانهم، وانتفخت بطونهم فأمرهم رسول الله الله أن يخرجوا إلى أموال الصدقات ويشربوا من أبوالها وألبانها ففعلوا وصحوا كذا في "المعدن. على الظاهر : وهو قوله علي: "اشربوا من أبوالها"، ويجعل هذا بذلك، أو يحمل هذا على تخصيص إباحته في أهل عرينة كذا في "المعدن". أصلاً: أي في جميع الأوقات لا للتدواي ولا لغيره، وهو مذهب أبي حنيفة له، والحديث حجة على أبي يوسف رحله في إباحة شربه للتداوي، وعلى محمد له في إباحة شربه وطهارته مطلقاً كذا في "المعدن".
* أخرجه البخاري في كتاب المحاربين من أهل الكفر والردة رقم ٦٤١٧ ، ومسلم في باب حكم المحاربين والمرتدين، رقم: ١٦٧١، وابن ماجه في باب من حارب وسعى في الأرض فساداً، رقم: ٢٥٧٨، عن أنس له. ** أخرجه أحمد في مسنده رقم ٩٠٤٧ ، عن أبي هريرة الله عنه عن النبي الله لو قال: إن أكثر عذاب القبر من البول، وابن ماجه، رقم: ٣٤٧، باب التشديد في البول عن أبي هريرة، والدار قطني ۱۲۸/۱، في باب نجاسة البول والأمر بالتنزه منه، والطبراني في الكبير ۱۱۱/۲۰، من حديث ابن عباس هما مرفوعاً، وقال الدار قطني: لا بأس به.

أخرجه البخاري في باب العشر فيما يسقى من ماء السماء وبالماء الجاري :رقم: ١٤۱۲، ومسلم في باب ما فيه العشر أو نصف العشر، رقم: ۹۸۱ عن جابر بن عبد الله أبو داود في باب صدقة الزروع، رقم: ١٥٩٦، عن عبد الله بن عمر، والترمذي في باب ما جاء في الصدقة فيما يسقى بالأنهار وغيره، رقم: ٦٣٩، عن أبي هريرة، والنسائي في باب ما يوجب العشر وما يوجب نصف العشر، رقم: ٢٤٨٨، عن عبد الله بن عمر، وابن ماجه في باب صدقة الزروع والثمار رقم ١٨١٦، عن أبي هريرة له.

البحث الأول في كتاب الله

وقوله عليه: "ليس في الخضروات صدقة * مؤوّل في نَفْيِ العُشْر؛ لأن الصَّدَقَةَ تَحتَملُ وجوهاً، فيترجَّحُ الأولُ على الثاني." ليس في الخضروات إلخ: روى الترمذي عن معاذ منه أنه كتب إلى النبي يسأله عن الخضروات وهي البقول، فقال: ليس فيها شيء، وضفعه الترمذي، وفيه الحسن بن عمارة ضعيف، ضعفه شعبة وغيره، وتركه ابن المبارك، وقال ابن حجر: متروك من السابقة كذا في "الفصول". تحتمل وجوهاً : فإنها كما تحتمل الزكاة والعشر، تحتمل غيره هما أيضاً كالتطوع، فإرادة العشر يكون بطريق التأويل، والمؤول غير قطعي، والنص قطعي، فيترجح. على الثاني: اعلم أنهم اختلفوا في زكاة الزروع والثمار ، فقال أبو حنيفة له كل ما فيه عادة ويقصد به اشتغال الأراضي ففيه العشر سواء كان مما يبقى سنة كالحنطة والشعير والتمر والزبيب أو لا يبقى كالبقول قليلاً كان أو كثيراً، وقال أبويوسف ومحمد والشافعي : لا عشر إلا فيما ثمرته باقية فيما بلغ خمسة أوسق لهم في اشتراط البقاء، وقوله علي: "ليس في الخضروات صدقة"، فإنه مطلقة يحتمل الزكاة والعشر، والزكاة غير منفية؛ لأنها يجب إذا بلغت قيمتها نصابا فتعين العشر، ولقوله عليها: "ما سقته السماء ففيه العشر"، فإنه نص في وجوب العشر في كل خارج باقياً كان أو غير باق وما رواه يحتمل بالعشرة والزكاة وغيرهما كالتطوع، وإنما أريد العشر بطريق التأويل كما ذكروا، والأول غير قطع لما قرر في محله والنص قطعي يترجح عليه كذا في "الفصول".
Section on contrasting terms. The preference is given to acting upon the nass. Similarly, his statement (peace be upon him) to the people of Urayna: 'Drink from their urine and their milk' is a nass regarding the cause of healing, and a zahir permitting drinking urine. And his statement (peace be upon him): 'Avoid urine, for indeed the majority of the punishment of the grave is from it' is a nass regarding the obligation of guarding against urine. Thus the nass is given preference over the zahir, so drinking urine is absolutely impermissible. And his statement (peace be upon him): 'What is watered by the sky, on it is the tithe' is a nass regarding the explanation of the tithe. So acting upon the nass is preferred because it is stronger than the zahir, as explained. [Al-Shafi: p. 90] 'From their urine': i.e., the urine of the camels of charity. It is narrated that a group from Urayna came to Medina, and the climate did not suit them, they fell ill, their colors turned yellow, and their stomachs swelled. So the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) ordered them to go out to the camels of charity and drink from their urine and milk, and they did so and recovered. As in 'Al-Ma'din. 'On the zahir': i.e., his statement (peace be upon him): 'Drink from their urine,' and this is made like that, or it is interpreted as a specification of its permissibility for the people of Urayna. As in 'Al-Ma'din. 'Absolutely': i.e., at all times, not for treatment or otherwise, and this is the madhhab of Abu Hanifa. The hadith is evidence against Abu Yusuf regarding the permissibility of drinking it for treatment, and against Muhammad regarding the permissibility of drinking it and its absolute purity. As in 'Al-Ma'din. * Narrated by Bukhari... ** Narrated by Ahmad... *** Narrated by Bukhari... The first section in the Book of Allah. Section on contrasting terms. And his statement (peace be upon him): 'There is no sadaqah on vegetables' is interpreted (mu'awwal) regarding the negation of the tithe, because 'sadaqah' can have multiple meanings. So the first (the nass) is given preference over the second. 'There is no sadaqah on vegetables' etc.: At-Tirmidhi narrated from Mu'adh... knows that they differed regarding the zakat of crops and fruits. Abu Hanifa said: Everything that is customarily and intentionally cultivated on land has the tithe, whether it remains for a year like wheat, barley, dates, and raisins, or does not remain like leafy vegetables, whether little or much. Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and Ash-Shafi'i said: There is no tithe except on what has lasting fruit and reaches five wasqs, due to their condition of remaining. And his statement: 'There is no sadaqah on vegetables' is general and can mean zakat or tithe, and zakat is not negated because it is obligatory when its value reaches a nisab, so the tithe is intended. And due to his statement: 'What is watered by the sky has the tithe,' which is a nass regarding the obligation of tithe on every produce, whether lasting or not. And what he narrated can mean tithe, zakat, or other things like voluntary charity, and the tithe is intended by way of ta'wil as they mentioned, and the first is not decisive as established in its place, and the nass is decisive and is given preference over it. As in 'Al-Fusul.'
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage from Usul al-Shashi addresses the principle that when a nass (unequivocal text) conflicts with a zahir (apparent but ambiguous text), the nass takes precedence. The author illustrates this with two Prophetic hadith: the first permits the Urayna tribe to drink camel urine for healing (a zahir in permissibility), while the second commands avoidance of urine (a nass on prohibition). The nass overrides the zahir, making urine impermissible absolutely. The commentary clarifies that the Urayna permission was a specific concession, not a general ruling. Similarly, the hadith on tithe (nass) is preferred over the hadith on vegetables (zahir) because the latter is open to interpretation. The passage then details the Hanafi position on zakat of crops: Abu Hanifa holds that all cultivated produce incurs tithe, while the Sahibayn and Shafi'i restrict it to staple, long-lasting crops. This dispute is resolved by prioritizing the nass (tithe hadith) over the mu'awwal (interpreted hadith on vegetables).

Key Terms
نص (nass): A text whose meaning is unequivocal and does not admit interpretation or specification.
ظاهر (zahir): A text whose apparent meaning is clear but may be subject to interpretation or specification.
مؤول (mu'awwal): A text that is interpreted away from its apparent meaning due to conflicting evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the principle explained in this lesson, what is the Hanafi ruling on drinking urine?
A) Permissible for treatment, as per the Urayna hadith.
B) Absolutely impermissible, because the avoidance hadith is a nass that overrides the Urayna hadith.
C) Permissible only if no other medicine is available.
D) Impermissible but the urine is considered pure.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, when a nass (clear, decisive text) conflicts with a zahir or mu'awwal, the nass is given preference, as demonstrated by the rulings on urine and tithe.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
