والخامس: وقد تترك الحقيقة بدلالة محلّ الكلام بأن كان المحل لا يقبل حقيقة اللفظ. حكم الطلاق ومثاله: انعقاد نكاح الحرة بلفظ البيع، والهبة، والتمليك، والصدقة، وقوله العبده وهو المولى أكبر سنا من المولى: معروف النسب من غيره: "هذا ابني" "وكذا إذا قال لعبده وهو " كان مجازاً عن العتق عند أبي حنيفة الا الله خلافاً لهما، بناءً على ما ذكرنا أن "هذا ابني" الصاحبين المجاز خلفٌ عن الحقيقة في حق اللفظ عنده عنده وفي حق الحكم عندهما. الصاحبين كان الحكم مقصوراً إلخ لأن الباعث على المنع من الخروج غضب آثاره فيه ما أرادت من الخروج بين يديه، فقيد قوله : إن خرجت إلخ بذلك الخروج كذا في "الفصول". بدلالة محل الكلام أي بدلالة ما وقع فيه الكلام وما يتعلق به بأن لا يكون صالحاً للمعنى الحقيقي إما للزوم الكذب فيمن هو معصوم عنه، أو لوجه آخر فإذا لم يقبل المحل المعنى الحقيقي، فيصار إلى المجاز لا محالة كقوله عليه: "إنما الأعمال بالنيات" فإن معناه الحقيقي أن لا توجد أعمال الجوارح إلا بالنية وهو كذب؛ لأن أكثر ما يقع العمل منا في وقت خلو الذهن عن النية، فلابد أن يحمل على المجاز :أي: ثواب الأعمال أو حكم الأعمال بالنيات، فإن قدر الثواب فظاهر أنه لا يدل على أن جواز الأعمال في الدنيا موقوف على النية، وإن قدر الحكم فهو :نوعان دنيوي كالصحة وأخروي كالثواب والعقاب والأخروي مراد بالاجماع بيننا وبين الشافعي محله، فلا يجوز أن يراد الدنيوي أيضاً أما عنده؛ فلأنه يلزم عموم المجاز، وأما عندنا؛ فلأنه يلزم عموم المشترك، فلا يدل على أن جواز العمل موقوف على النية، فلا تكون النية فرضاً في الوضوء فتذكر. مثاله إلخ: أي إذا قال الحرة: "بعت نفسي منك" صار مجازاً عن النكاح؛ لأن حقيقة الكلام أعني تمليك الرقبة لا يحتمله الحرة، فترك إلى المجاز، وكذلك قوله لعبده المعروف النسب عن غيره، أو الأكبر سناً منه: "هذا ابني"؛ لأن العبد الثابت النسب من زيد لا يحتمل أن يكون من عمرو مثلاً، وكذا الأكبر سناً لا يحتمل أن يكون ابناً للأصغر سناً، فترك حقيقة اللفظ كذا في "الشرح". وقوله لعبده إلخ إنما أورد هذا ليفهم أن المراد بعدم القابلية في المحل بحقيقة الكلام أعم من أن يكون عقلياً أو شرعياً.
The fifth: And the literal meaning may be abandoned due to the indication of the context of the speech, such that the context does not accept the literal meaning of the word. Example: the contract of marriage of a free woman using the words of sale, gift, ownership, and charity; and his statement to his slave who is older than the master or of known lineage from someone else: 'This is my son.' Similarly, if he says to his slave and he was—it is metaphorical for manumission according to Abu Hanifa, contrary to the two companions, based on what we mentioned: that metaphor is a substitute for literal meaning in terms of wording according to Abu Hanifa, and in terms of ruling according to the two companions. The ruling was confined to the state, etc., because the motive for preventing going out was anger that had its effect in her wanting to go out in his presence, so he restricted his statement 'If you go out, etc.' to that going out, as in al-Fusul. 'By indication of the context of speech' means by indication of what the speech occurs in and what relates to it, such that the context is not suitable for the literal meaning, either due to necessitating a lie from one who is infallible, or for another reason. So if the context does not accept the literal meaning, then recourse to metaphor is inevitable, like his statement (peace be upon him): 'Actions are but by intentions.' For its literal meaning is that no actions of the limbs exist except with intention, and that is false, because most of our actions occur when the mind is free of intention. So it must be interpreted metaphorically: meaning, the reward of actions or the ruling of actions are by intentions. If it is interpreted as reward, then clearly it does not indicate that the permissibility of actions in this world is dependent on intention. If it is interpreted as ruling, then it is of two types: worldly like validity, and otherworldly like reward and punishment. The otherworldly is agreed upon between us and al-Shafi'i, so it is not permissible to intend the worldly also. According to him, because it would entail general metaphor; according to us, because it would entail general homonymy. So it does not indicate that the permissibility of action depends on intention, thus intention is not obligatory (fardh) in wudu, so remember. Example etc.: i.e., if a free woman says, 'I have sold myself to you,' it becomes metaphorical for marriage, because the literal meaning—i.e., ownership of the person—cannot apply to a free woman, so recourse is to metaphor. Similarly, his statement to his slave who is of known lineage from someone else, or older in age: 'This is my son,' because the slave whose lineage is established from Zaid cannot be from Amr, and the older cannot be the son of the younger, so the literal meaning is abandoned, as in al-Sharh. And his statement to his slave, etc., is mentioned to make understood that the intention by 'lack of acceptance' in the context of the literal meaning includes both rational and legal deficiency.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage from Usul al-Shashi discusses the fifth reason for abandoning literal meaning: the context of the speech (mahall al-kalam) itself does not allow the literal interpretation. It opens with examples: a free woman's marriage contract using words of sale, gift, etc., cannot literally mean transfer of ownership, so it is interpreted as marriage (majaz). Similarly, saying 'This is my son' to a slave who is older than the master or whose lineage is known from another person cannot be literal, so it is metaphorical—for Abu Hanifa, it is a metaphor for manumission. The text then examines the famous hadith 'Actions are but by intentions.' Its literal meaning would imply no action occurs without intention, which is false because many actions happen without premeditated intention (e.g., habitual actions). Therefore, the hadith must be interpreted metaphorically: either reward or the ruling (hukm) of actions depends on intention. The ruling is further divided into worldly (e.g., validity) and otherworldly (reward/punishment). Both Hanafis and Shafi'is agree the otherworldly aspect is intended. The dispute arises about whether the worldly aspect is also intended: Shafi'is argue validity requires intention (leading to niyyah being fard in wudu), while Hanafis argue that interpreting it as worldly would entail either 'general metaphor' (for Shafi'is) or 'general homonymy' (for Hanafis), neither of which is acceptable. Thus, the hadith does not prove that intention is obligatory in acts of worship like wudu. The passage ends by clarifying that the inacceptability of literal meaning can be due to rational impossibility (like absurdity) or legal impossibility (like a free woman being owned).

Key Terms
مجاز (majaz): Metaphorical meaning used when the literal meaning is impossible or inappropriate; a substitute for haqiqah.
محل الكلام (mahall al-kalam): The context or subject of the speech that may preclude the literal meaning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is it permissible to abandon literal meaning?
A) When the literal meaning is inconvenient
B) When the context does not accept the literal meaning due to impossibility or falsehood
C) Whenever the speaker intends a metaphor
D) Only for the hadith of intentions

Key Takeaway
When the context cannot accept literal meaning, metaphor becomes the valid interpretation; this principle is foundational in Hanafi usul.

The Hadith of Intentions: Hanafi Analysis and Dispute with Shafi'i
Analyzes the hadith of intentions to derive that intention is not fard in wudu.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that the hadith 'إنما الأعمال بالنيات' does not make intention obligatory in acts like wudu, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
The hadith's literal meaning is false; when interpreted metaphorically, it cannot prove worldly validity requires intention.

This lesson delves into the application of the principle from the previous lesson to the famous hadith 'Actions are but by intentions'. The passage from Usul al-Shashi demonstrates how the literal meaning is impossible because many actions occur without premeditated intention. Therefore, the hadith must be metaphorical. We then examine the dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i: does the hadith require intention for the validity of acts like wudu? The Hanafi school argues it does not.

Guided Path
1
Identify the literal meaning
The hadith would mean that no physical action exists except with intention. That is false because actions like habitual gestures occur without intention.
The passage says 'وهو كذب؛ لأن أكثر ما يقع العمل منا في وقت خلو الذهن عن النية'.
2
Apply the principle of abandoning literal meaning
Since the context (common experience and infallibility of the Prophet) cannot accept the literal meaning, we move to metaphor.
3
Determine possible metaphorical meanings
Two possibilities: reward of actions (thawab) or ruling (hukm) of actions. Ruling splits into worldly (validity) and otherworldly (reward/punishment).
4
Test the possibility of including worldly validity
Both schools agree otherworldly is intended. For worldly, Shafi'i says it is included via generality, but Hanafis object that this leads to 'umum al-majaz' (general metaphor) or 'umum al-mushtarak' (general homonymy), which are invalid in Hanafi usul.
5
Conclude the Hanafi ruling
The hadith does not prove that intention is fard in wudu. Niyyah is not required for validity in the Hanafi school.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf.

Commentary
This passage from Usul al-Shashi discusses the fifth reason for abandoning literal meaning: the context of the speech (mahall al-kalam) itself does not allow the literal interpretation. It opens with examples: a free woman's marriage contract using words of sale, gift, etc., cannot literally mean transfer of ownership, so it is interpreted as marriage (majaz). Similarly, saying 'This is my son' to a slave who is older than the master or whose lineage is known from another person cannot be literal, so it is metaphorical—for Abu Hanifa, it is a metaphor for manumission. The text then examines the famous hadith 'Actions are but by intentions.' Its literal meaning would imply no action occurs without intention, which is false because many actions happen without premeditated intention (e.g., habitual actions). Therefore, the hadith must be interpreted metaphorically: either reward or the ruling (hukm) of actions depends on intention. The ruling is further divided into worldly (e.g., validity) and otherworldly (reward/punishment). Both Hanafis and Shafi'is agree the otherworldly aspect is intended. The dispute arises about whether the worldly aspect is also intended: Shafi'is argue validity requires intention (leading to niyyah being fard in wudu), while Hanafis argue that interpreting it as worldly would entail either 'general metaphor' (for Shafi'is) or 'general homonymy' (for Hanafis), neither of which is acceptable. Thus, the hadith does not prove that intention is obligatory in acts of worship like wudu. The passage ends by clarifying that the inacceptability of literal meaning can be due to rational impossibility (like absurdity) or legal impossibility (like a free woman being owned).

Key Terms
مجاز (majaz): Metaphorical meaning used when the literal meaning is impossible or inappropriate; a substitute for haqiqah.
عموم المجاز (umum al-majaz): Applying a metaphorical meaning to all instances; considered problematic in Hanafi usul because metaphor is not originally general.
عموم المشترك (umum al-mushtarak): Applying a homonym (word with multiple meanings) to all meanings; rejected in Hanafi usul.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school hold that the hadith 'إنما الأعمال بالنيات' does not make intention fard in wudu?
A) Because the hadith is weak in transmission
B) Because the literal meaning is false, and the metaphorical meaning cannot include worldly validity without causing 'umum al-majaz' or 'umum al-mushtarak'
C) Because wudu is not considered an 'action' (amal)
D) Because the hadith was abrogated

Key Takeaway
The hadith of intentions proves niyyah is required for otherworldly reward, not for the validity of acts like wudu.

Dalaalah al-Isharah from the Muhajirun Verse
Explaining how a Quranic verse indicates a ruling via isharah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi concept of dalaalah al-isharah to derive a ruling from a Quranic text and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
A term taken literally in context can yield a ruling not explicitly stated — this is dalaalah al-isharah.

This passage introduces a powerful tool of Hanafi usul: deriving rulings from texts not through explicit wording (ibarah) but through contextual implication (isharah). The Quranic description of the Muhajirun as 'poor' yields a ruling on the ownership of seized property. Let's see how the Hanafis extract this ruling and why the Shafi'is disagree.

Guided Path
1
Identify the text and its literal meaning
Read the verse: 'لِلْفُقَرَاءِ الْمُهَاجِرِينَ...' (Surah al-Hashr 59:8). The term 'fugaraa' (poor) is applied to the Muhajirun. Determine the literal (haqiqah) meaning of 'faqir'.
In Hanafi usul, 'faqir' literally means one who owns nothing.
2
Check the context for consistency
The same verse says they were 'أخرجوا من ديارهم وأموالهم' (driven from their homes and properties). This shows they owned property before hijra. So their poverty cannot be original non-ownership.
This contextual evidence is critical for the istinbat.
3
Apply the principle of istishar (indication)
Since the literal meaning of 'poor' is absence of ownership, and they previously had property, their poverty must be due to loss of ownership. The only plausible cause is seizure by the disbelievers.
Isharah works here because the literal meaning and context align to give a legal implication.
4
Derive the ruling via isharah
Thus, the verse indicates that when a disbeliever seizes Muslim property in dar al-harb, it becomes his property. This is the derived ruling.
The ruling is not explicitly stated (ibarah) but inferred (isharah).
5
Compare with Shafi'i position and defend Hanafi view
Al-Shafi'i interprets 'fugaraa' metaphorically (majaz) as a description of state, thus no legal implication. The Hanafi response: literal meaning is default; turning to metaphorical is contrary to principle without evidence.
This highlights the importance of usul principles: asl fi al-kalam al-haqiqah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البحث الأول في كتاب الله V. فصل في متعلقات النصوص لبيان استحقاق الغنيمة، فصار نصاً في ذلك، وقد ثبت فقرهم بنظم النص، فكان لبيان مستحقها بطريق الإشارة المهاجرين. إشارةً إلى أن استيلاء الكافر على مال المسلم سبب لثبوت الملك للكافر؛ إذ لو كانت غلبة الأموال باقيةً على ملكهم لا يثبتُ فَقْرُهم. أموال المسلمين المهاجرين الكفار ويُخَرَّج منه الحكمُ في مسألة الاستيلاء، وحكم ثبوت الملك للتاجر بالشراء منهم وتصرفاته من البيع والهبة والإعتاق، وحكمُ ثبوت الاستغنام، وثبوت الملك للغازي، وعجز المالك عن انتزاعه من يده، وتفريعاته. القدم ي الغازي ذلك يرى من كان وهو إنسان إنساناً بقصد نظره، ومع عن يمينه وشماله بمؤق عينينه من غير التفات وقصد، فالأول بمنزلة العبارة والثاني بمنزلة الإشارة كذا في كتب الأصول. وقد ثبت فقرهم إلخ: وهو قوله تعالى: الْفُقَرَاءِ الْمُهَاجرين الآية، لا يقال بأنه جاز أن يكون إطلاق الفقراء عليهم باعتبار العدم الأصلي بأن لم يكن لهم أموال؛ لأنه قد كانت لهم أموال بمكة بدليل قوله تعالى : الَّذِينَ أُخْرِجُوا مِنْ دِيَارِهِمْ وَأَمْوَالِهِمْ ﴾ الآية. لا يثبتُ فَقْرُهم: لأن الفقير حقيقة يكون بزوال الملك لا يبعد اليد عن المال مع قيام الملك؛ لأن ضده الغناء ملك المال لأقرب اليد منه، وإذا كان الفقير عديم الملك تسميتهم فقراء دليلاً على زوال ملكهم إلى الكفار؛ لأن مطلق الكلام محمول على حقيقته. والشافعي رحله لم يعمل بهذا الإشارة قائلاً بأن الله تعالى سماهم فقراء مجازاً، لكنا نقول: صرف الكلام إلى المجاز مع إمكان العمل بالحقيقة خلاف الأصل، فلا يصار إليه من غير ضرورة ودليل يصرف إليه كذا في "المعدن". في مسألة الاستيلاء يعني أن الكافر إذا استولى على مال المسلمين فأحرزه بدار الحرب يصير ملكا له عندنا خلافا للشافعي له، وفي بعض النسخ الاستيلاد ومعناه: أن الكافر إذا استولى جارية المسلم واستولدها يثبت النسب منه بناء على ما ذكر، والمشهور هو نسخة الاستيلاء. وحكم ثبوت الملك: أي حكم هو ثبوت الملك للتاجر بالشراء منهم أي من الكفار عطفاً تفسيرياً لقوله: الحكم في مسألة الاستيلاء يعني أن التاجر إذا اشترى من الكفار بعد استيلائهم على أموالنا ثبت له الملك كذا في "المعدن". وتفريعاته بالرفع عطف على الحكم وأراد بها مثل حل الوطء، وجواز الإعتاق، وعدم الضمان عند الإتلاف وغير ذلك.
The first research concerning the Book of Allah. V. Section on the dependents of texts. For the explanation of the entitlement to the spoils, so it became a nass in that, and their poverty was established by the arrangement of the nass, so it was to explain its recipient by way of isharah: the Muhajirun. An indication that a disbeliever's seizure of a Muslim's property is a cause for ownership to be established for the disbeliever; for if the properties had remained in their ownership, their poverty would not be established. The properties of the Muslims, the Muhajirun, the disbelievers. And from it is derived the ruling concerning the matter of seizure, and the ruling of ownership being established for the merchant by purchasing from them and his dispositions such as sale, gift, and manumission, and the ruling of entitlement to spoil, and ownership for the warrior, and the owner's inability to retrieve it from his hand, and its ramifications. The foot [؟] of the warrior. That is seen concerning one who is a human looking with intention of his gaze, and with his right and left with the corner of his two eyes without turning and intention, so the first is like ibarah and the second is like isharah. As in the books of usul. 'And their poverty was established' etc.: It is His saying: 'the poor Muhajirun' verse. It cannot be said that maybe the application of 'poor' to them is due to original non-existence in that they had no property; because they had property in Mecca, as evidenced by His saying: 'Those who were driven from their homes and their property.' Their poverty is not established: because the faqir in reality is one whose ownership ceases, not that his hand is distant from wealth while ownership remains; because its opposite, ghina, is ownership of wealth with proximity of hand. And if the faqir is one without ownership, their being called poor is evidence of transfer of their ownership to the disbelievers; because unrestricted speech is taken in its literal meaning. Al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him) did not act upon this isharah, saying that Allah called them poor metaphorically. But we say: turning speech from literal to metaphorical while literal is possible opposes the principle, so it is not resorted to without necessity and evidence that turns it. As in 'al-Ma'din'. In the matter of seizure: meaning that if a disbeliever seizes the property of a Muslim and takes possession of it in dar al-harb, it becomes his property according to us, contrary to al-Shafi'i. In some copies 'istilad' and its meaning: if a disbeliever seizes a Muslim's slave-girl and impregnates her, lineage is established from him based on what was mentioned, and the well-known version is 'istila''. And the ruling of ownership: meaning the ruling of ownership for the merchant by purchasing from them, i.e. from the disbelievers, as an explanatory apposition to His statement. The ruling in the matter of seizure means that if a merchant buys from the disbelievers after their seizure of our property, ownership is established for him. As in 'al-Ma'din'. And its ramifications: in the nominative case, it is conjunction on 'al-hukm', and it intends examples like permissibility of intercourse, permissibility of emancipation, and non-liability upon destruction, etc.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of dalaalah al-isharah (indication by signal) in the Quran. The author uses the verse about the Muhajirun (the Emigrants) to show how a ruling is derived from a term used in a context. The term 'fugaraa' (poor) is applied to the Muhajirun. Since they had property in Mecca (as the verse says they were driven from their homes and properties), their poverty after hijra cannot be original non-ownership. Therefore, their poverty must be due to loss of ownership to the disbelievers who seized their property. This gives a ruling via isharah: if a disbeliever seizes Muslim property in dar al-harb, it becomes his property. The passage also distinguishes ibarah (explicit statement) from isharah (indirect indication). The Shafi'i view is that the term 'poor' is metaphorical, but the Hanafi response is that literal meaning is default and cannot be abandoned without necessity. From this ruling, further rulings are derived: a merchant buying from disbelievers after seizure gets ownership, the warrior gets ownership of spoils, etc. The footnotes clarify nuances and variant readings.

Key Terms
إشارة (isharah): A type of dalaalah where the ruling is not explicitly stated but inferred from the wording or context without being the primary intention.
عبارة (ibarah): A type of dalaalah where the ruling is explicitly stated in the text directly.
فقير (faqir): One who does not own property; literally someone without wealth.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view in this passage, what does the term 'fugaraa' in the Muhajirun verse indicate?
A) That the Muhajirun were originally poor
B) That they lost their property to the disbelievers, implying transfer of ownership
C) That they were metaphorically poor in spirit
D) That they had no property even in Mecca

Key Takeaway
Dalaalah al-isharah allows us to derive rulings from a text's literal meaning combined with its context, and the Hanafi school defends this by the principle that literal meaning is primary.

Deriving Rulings from the Verse: Wet Dream, Cupping, and the Intention Dispute
Deriving rulings from the verse and the tabyit dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of textual indication to derive rulings on wet dream and cupping, and evaluate the tabyit dispute by distinguishing between Quran and solitary hadith.

Core Idea
The verse 'complete the fast' defines fasting by abstention; wet dream does not break it, and tabyit is not required because the command applies after dawn.

This passage from Usul al-Shashi uses the Quranic verse 'Then complete the fast' (2:187) to extract multiple fiqh rulings and engage in a key usul dispute. By examining the verse's linguistic scope, the author demonstrates how wet dream and cupping are not nullifiers of the fast, and then tackles the heated debate on whether intention (niyyah) must be formed from the night. Understanding this derivation will sharpen your ability to read the Quran as a source of law and to weigh textual evidence against hadith reports.

Guided Path
1
Locate the proof text
Identify the primary verse and the hadith cited in the passage. The verse is 'Then complete the fast until the night' (2:187). The hadith is 'The cupper and the cupped break the fast' and 'No fasting for one who does not intend from the night.'
The verse defines fasting as abstaining from eating, drinking, and sexual relations from dawn to night.
2
Derive the ruling on wet dream
The passage argues that since the verse does not mention wet dream, it does not break the fast. The reasoning is based on a fortiori argument: if voluntary janabah does not break the fast, then involuntary wet dream is even less likely to do so.
The key is the linguistic definition: the verse only prohibits specific acts; wet dream is not one of them.
3
Interpret the cupping hadith
The hadith 'The cupper and the cupped break the fast' is interpreted as 'approaching breaking' due to weakness or risk of ingestion, not actual nullification. The Hanafi school harmonizes it with the Quranic ruling.
This shows the principle that solitary hadith (khabar wahid) cannot abrogate the Quran; they must be interpreted in light of the stronger text.
4
Analyze the tabyit dispute
Identify the two positions: Hanafi (intention before noon suffices) vs. Shafi'i (intention from night required). The Shafi'i proof is the hadith. The Hanafi proof is the verse itself: 'complete' implies the fast has already started, so the command applies after dawn.
The Hanafi response is that accepting the Shafi'i hadith would mean abrogating the Quran with a solitary report, which is impermissible.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school acts by both texts: the verse permits intention after dawn, and the hadith is interpreted as recommending (not requiring) intention from the night. Thus both are acted upon, avoiding naskh.
This is a classic example of jam' (reconciliation) in usul al-fiqh.

Source Text

The first research in the Book of Allah. Section on the implications of texts. And it is known from it, i.e., the statement of Allah 'Then complete the fast', the ruling on wet dream, cupping, and oiling. Because the Book, since it called the necessary abstention by refraining from the three mentioned things at the beginning of dawn 'fasting', it is known that the pillar of fasting is accomplished by refraining from those three things. And upon this, the ruling on tabyit (intending at night) is derived. For the intention to perform the commanded act is only incumbent at the time of performing it which is the commanded fast, and the command only applies after the first part, as Allah says: 'Then complete the fast until the night.' And as for dalaalat al-nass (indication of the text): it is what is known as the effective cause of the ruled matter linguistically, not by ijtihad or derivation. It is known from the commentary: As for wet dream, it does not break the fast because you already know that janabah from one's wife, which is voluntary, does not conflict with fasting, so janabah from a wet dream is even more so, and necessity is greater due to sleep. And the hadith 'The cupper and the cupped break the fast' is interpreted: they approached breaking the fast; the cupped because of blood loss causing weakness, and the cupper because he cannot be safe from something entering his throat. As for the issue of tabyit: whether Ramadan fast requires intention from the night. According to al-Shafi'i, it is required due to his statement: 'No fasting for one who does not intend from the night.' According to our companions, it is not required; rather, it is fulfilled by intention before noon. This is because intention is resolve. If we said it is not valid from the night as al-Shafi'i said, it would lead to abrogating the Book with a solitary report. So we permitted it in both cases, acting by both Book and Sunnah. Then the explanation: the intention linguistically is resolve, and the resolve to perform the commanded act only occurs when the command exists, and the command applies only after the first part, so prior intention from the night is not necessary. The verse 'then complete the fast until the night' is a command to complete, and completion can only be after commencement. 'Linguistically' distinguishes it from the 'proportion' in his statement 'known' — that is, known linguistically: i.e., the effective meaning is known to one who is an expert in the Arabic language, whether a jurist or not. It excludes implication (iqtida) and ellipsis (mahdhuf) because they are established by revelation or reason. And his statement 'not by ijtihad' emphasizes 'linguistically'. It is a refutation of those who claim that dalaalat al-nass is qiyas but hidden, while this indication is clear. How can this be when qiyas is conjectural known only by a mujtahid, while this indication is definitive known by every native speaker? Moreover, this indication was legislated before qiyas was established, and no one denies it.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage analyzes the verse 'Then complete the fast until the night' (Quran 2:187) to derive multiple rulings and a usul principle. First, the verse defines fasting as abstaining from three things (eating, drinking, sexual relations); anything else, like wet dream, cupping, or oiling, is not breaking the fast. The wet dream is even less problematic than voluntary janabah. The hadith about cupping is interpreted as only approaching breaking the fast due to weakness. Second, the issue of tabyit (intention from the night) is addressed: the Hanafi position is that intention (niyyah) is not required from the night because the command to 'complete' implies the fast has already begun, and the command only applies after the first part of dawn. The Shafi'i position, based on a hadith, is rejected because it would abrogate the Quran with a solitary report. Third, the definition of dalaalat al-nass (textual indication) is given: it is the recognition of the effective cause (illah) of a ruling directly from the language, not through ijtihad or qiyas. This is contrasted with qiyas, which is conjectural. The passage emphasizes that dalaalat al-nass is definitive and known to any Arabic speaker, unlike qiyas which requires specialized training. The author refutes those who equate dalaalat al-nass with qiyas.

Key Terms
التبييت (al-tabyit): Making intention for fasting during the night, i.e., before dawn.
الاحتلام (al-ihtilam): Wet dream; involuntary emission of semen during sleep.
الاحتجام (al-ihitijam): Cupping; a medical procedure involving bloodletting.

Key Takeaway
The verse 'Then complete the fast' defines the fast by abstaining from three specific acts; any other act, such as wet dream or cupping, does not break it, and intention is not required from the night.

Dalaalat al-Nass: Textual Indication as a Definitive Proof
Defining dalaalat al-nass and distinguishing it from qiyas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and define dalaalat al-nass, distinguish it from qiyas, and apply its conditions to a given text.

Core Idea
Dalaalat al-nass is a definitive textual indication known to every Arabic speaker, not requiring ijtihad.

In this passage, the author introduces a key usul concept: dalaalat al-nass (textual indication). This is the recognition of the effective cause (illah) of a ruling directly from the language of the text, without resorting to ijtihad or qiyas. The concept is crucial for understanding how Hanafi scholars prioritize evidence. We will dissect its definition, conditions, and contrast with qiyas.

Guided Path
1
Define dalaalat al-nass
Extract the definition from the text: it is what is known as the effective cause (illah) of a ruling from the language itself, not through ijtihad or derivation.
The key phrases: 'ma ulima illatan' (what is known as the cause) and 'lughatan' (linguistically).
2
Identify its conditions
The condition is that the effective cause must be understood directly from the linguistic structure, by any Arabic speaker, without needing legal reasoning.
The author emphasizes 'lughatan' and contrasts with 'ijtihad' and 'istinbat'.
3
Distinguish from qiyas
The passage explicitly refutes those who equate dalaalat al-nass with qiyas. Qiyas is conjectural (zanni) and requires specialized knowledge, while dalaalat al-nass is definitive (qati) and accessible to all native speakers.
The author calls the equation 'how can this be?' (kayfa yakunu hadha).
4
Identify its scope
Dalaalat al-nass is legislated before qiyas and is not disputed. It is a stronger form of proof that does not require analogy.
The text mentions it was established before qiyas (qabla shar' al-qiyas).
5
Apply to an example
Use the verse 'Then complete the fast until the night' as an example: the word 'complete' (atimmu) linguistically implies a pre-existing state, indicating that the fast starts at dawn. This is dalaalat al-nass.
This example is from the same passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

عنه : "أفطر الحاجم والمحجوم"؛ فتأويله: أنهما قربا بالإفطار المحجوم بسبب خروج الدم المورث للضعف، والحاجم بسبب أنه لا يأمن من دخول شيء في جوفه كذا في "الحصول". في مسألة التبييت وهي أن صوم رمضان هل تشترط فيه التبييت أي: النية من الليل أم لا، فعند الشافعي حه يشترط؛ لقوله عليه: "لا صيام لمن لم ينو الصيام من الليل"، وعند أصحابنا لا يشترط بل يتأدى بنيته قبل الزوال؛ وهذا لأن النية هي القصد، فلو قلنا: إنه لا يجوز من الليل كما هي قال الشافعي الله لأدى إلى نسخ الكتاب بخبر الواحد، فقلنا بالجواز فيهما عملاً بالكتاب والسنة جميعاً. ثُمَّ أَتِمُّوا الصيام. إلخ: ومحصوله: أن النية لغة: القصد، وقصد إتيان المأمور به لا يكون إلا إذا يوجد الطلب، والخطاب لإتيان المأمور به والخطاب إنما يتوجه في الصوم بعد الجزء الأول، فلا يلزم تقدم النية عليه من الليل كما ذهب إليه الشافعي . إِلَى اللَّيْلِ إلخ: فإن قوله تعالى: ثُمَّ أَتِمُّوا الصِّيَامَ إِلَى اللَّيْلِ أمر لإتمام الصيام، والإتمام لا يكون إلا بعد الشروع. لغة تمييز عن النسبة في قوله: علم أي علم ذلك من حيث اللغة :أي: يعرف المعنى المؤثر من هو عارف بلغة العرب، سواء كان فقيهاً أو غير فقيه، ويخرج به الاقتضاء والمحذوف؛ لأنهما ثابتان شرعاً أو عقلاً، وقوله: "لا اجتهاداً" تأكيد لقوله: "لغة"، وفيه رد على من زعم أن دلالة النص هو القياس لكنه خفي والدلالة جلي وكيف يكون هذا والقياس ظني لا يقف عليه إلا المجتهد، والدلالة قطعية يعرفها كل من كان من أهل اللسان، وأيضاً كانت هي مشروعة قبل شرع القياس ولا ينكرها منكر.
The first research in the Book of Allah. Section on the implications of texts. And it is known from it, i.e., the statement of Allah 'Then complete the fast', the ruling on wet dream, cupping, and oiling. Because the Book, since it called the necessary abstention by refraining from the three mentioned things at the beginning of dawn 'fasting', it is known that the pillar of fasting is accomplished by refraining from those three things. And upon this, the ruling on tabyit (intending at night) is derived. For the intention to perform the commanded act is only incumbent at the time of performing it which is the commanded fast, and the command only applies after the first part, as Allah says: 'Then complete the fast until the night.' And as for dalaalat al-nass (indication of the text): it is what is known as the effective cause of the ruled matter linguistically, not by ijtihad or derivation. It is known from the commentary: As for wet dream, it does not break the fast because you already know that janabah from one's wife, which is voluntary, does not conflict with fasting, so janabah from a wet dream is even more so, and necessity is greater due to sleep. And the hadith 'The cupper and the cupped break the fast' is interpreted: they approached breaking the fast; the cupped because of blood loss causing weakness, and the cupper because he cannot be safe from something entering his throat. As for the issue of tabyit: whether Ramadan fast requires intention from the night. According to al-Shafi'i, it is required due to his statement: 'No fasting for one who does not intend from the night.' According to our companions, it is not required; rather, it is fulfilled by intention before noon. This is because intention is resolve. If we said it is not valid from the night as al-Shafi'i said, it would lead to abrogating the Book with a solitary report. So we permitted it in both cases, acting by both Book and Sunnah. Then the explanation: the intention linguistically is resolve, and the resolve to perform the commanded act only occurs when the command exists, and the command applies only after the first part, so prior intention from the night is not necessary. The verse 'then complete the fast until the night' is a command to complete, and completion can only be after commencement. 'Linguistically' distinguishes it from the 'proportion' in his statement 'known' — that is, known linguistically: i.e., the effective meaning is known to one who is an expert in the Arabic language, whether a jurist or not. It excludes implication (iqtida) and ellipsis (mahdhuf) because they are established by revelation or reason. And his statement 'not by ijtihad' emphasizes 'linguistically'. It is a refutation of those who claim that dalaalat al-nass is qiyas but hidden, while this indication is clear. How can this be when qiyas is conjectural known only by a mujtahid, while this indication is definitive known by every native speaker? Moreover, this indication was legislated before qiyas was established, and no one denies it.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage analyzes the verse 'Then complete the fast until the night' (Quran 2:187) to derive multiple rulings and a usul principle. First, the verse defines fasting as abstaining from three things (eating, drinking, sexual relations); anything else, like wet dream, cupping, or oiling, is not breaking the fast. The wet dream is even less problematic than voluntary janabah. The hadith about cupping is interpreted as only approaching breaking the fast due to weakness. Second, the issue of tabyit (intention from the night) is addressed: the Hanafi position is that intention (niyyah) is not required from the night because the command to 'complete' implies the fast has already begun, and the command only applies after the first part of dawn. The Shafi'i position, based on a hadith, is rejected because it would abrogate the Quran with a solitary report. Third, the definition of dalaalat al-nass (textual indication) is given: it is the recognition of the effective cause (illah) of a ruling directly from the language, not through ijtihad or qiyas. This is contrasted with qiyas, which is conjectural. The passage emphasizes that dalaalat al-nass is definitive and known to any Arabic speaker, unlike qiyas which requires specialized training. The author refutes those who equate dalaalat al-nass with qiyas.

Key Terms
دلالة النص (dalaalat al-nass): Textual indication that reveals the effective cause (illah) of a ruling directly from language, not by ijtihad or qiyas.
الاجتهاد (al-ijtihad): Independent legal reasoning to derive a ruling from the sources.
القياس (al-qiyas): Analogical reasoning, transferring a ruling from an original case to a new case due to a shared effective cause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the key difference between dalaalat al-nass and qiyas according to the passage?
A) Dalaalat al-nass is based on the Quran, while qiyas is based on the Sunnah.
B) Dalaalat al-nass is definitive (qati) and linguistically understood, while qiyas is conjectural (zanni) and requires ijtihad.
C) Dalaalat al-nass was invented after qiyas.
D) There is no difference; they are synonymous.

Key Takeaway
Dalaalat al-nass is a definitive textual indication of the effective cause, known linguistically, and must be distinguished from conjectural qiyas.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
8 min
Implication of a Command and Absence of Generality
Explaining that implied commands lack generality and cannot be specified

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi principle that an implied command (muqtada) has no generality and cannot be specified, and apply it to determine that saying 'Observe the waiting period' with intent results in one revocable divorce.

Core Idea
An implication (muqtada) establishes only what is necessary, not a general rule.

In Hanafi usul, what is established by necessity (muqtada) is strictly limited. This lesson explores a classic example from Usul al-Shashi: a husband tells his wife to observe the waiting period, intending divorce. The implication is that divorce has occurred, but only the bare minimum is established.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle
Read the passage and locate the statement that the implied command (muqtada) does not have generality. This is the core rule.
The phrase 'ولا عموم للمقتضى' is the key.
2
Understand the link to specification
Specification (takhsis) depends on generality (umum). Since the implied command has no generality, it cannot be specified.
3
Analyze the example
Consider the husband's statement 'Observe the waiting period' (اعتدّي) after consummation, with the intention of divorce. Determine what is explicitly said and what is implied.
The waiting period is only required after divorce, so divorce is implied out of necessity.
4
Determine the scope of the implication
Only the necessary elements of the implication are established. Here, the necessity is that divorce has occurred, but only one revocable divorce is required for the waiting period to be valid.
Irrevocability (البينونة) is extra and not necessary, so it is not established by implication.
5
Derive the ruling
The ruling is that one revocable divorce occurs. No more, no less.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الأمر ترتفع بالفرد المطلق، ولا تخصيص في الفرد المطلق؛ لأن التخصيص يعتمد العموم، ولو ولا عموم للمقتضى قال بعد الدخول: "اعتَدي" ونوى به الطلاق، فيقع الطلاق اقتضاء؛ لأن الاعتداد يقتضي وجود الطلاق، فيقدَّر الطلاق موجوداً ضرورة. الزوج قعودها للعدة ولهذا كان الواقع به رجعياً؛ لأن صفة البينونة زائدة على قدر الضرورة، فلا يثبت بطريق الاقتضاء، ولا يقع إلا واحد لما؛ ذكرنا.
Chapter on Command: It is lifted by the absolute individual, and there is no specification in the absolute individual because specification depends on generality, and there is no generality for an implication. He said after entry: 'Observe the waiting period' and intended divorce by it; then divorce occurs by implication because observing the waiting period necessitates the existence of divorce, so divorce is considered to exist necessarily. The husband, her sitting for the waiting period. Hence, what occurs is a revocable divorce because the attribute of irrevocability is extra to the extent of necessity, so it is not established by way of implication, and only one occurs for the reason we mentioned.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage explains the principle that what is established by implication (مقتضى) does not have general applicability and cannot be specified. The example is a husband telling his wife 'اعتدّي' (observe the waiting period) after consummation, intending divorce by that statement. Since the waiting period can only be after divorce, the implication is that divorce has occurred. However, only the minimum necessary is established: one revocable divorce, not an irrevocable one, because irrevocability is additional and not necessary. This is a Hanafi principle: the implied command is restricted to what is necessary.

Key Terms
المقتضى (al-muqtada): The meaning implied by a statement without being explicitly mentioned, established by necessity.
الفرد المطلق (al-fard al-mutlaq): The absolute individual, a single instance without restriction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example given, when a husband says 'Observe the waiting period' after consummation intending divorce, how many divorces occur and what type?
A) One revocable divorce
B) One irrevocable divorce
C) Three revocable divorces
D) No divorce

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, what is established by implication (muqtada) is limited to the bare necessity; it has no generality and cannot be specified.
